This prophecy was partly fulfilled during Isaiah's time or soon after. The complete fulfillment will occur when the Messiah (God's chosen one) arrives to begin and fully establish the age to come.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 11:6

## Isaiah 11:9 (id: 15850)

Nothing will hurt or destroy: There will be no evildoers, corruption, or sin on God’s holy mountain (Zion).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 11:9

## Isaiah 11:10 (id: 15839)

the heir to David’s throne: Literally *the root of Jesse* ([11:1](https://ref.ly/Isa11:1); [Rev 5:5](https://ref.ly/Rev5:5); [22:16](https://ref.ly/Rev22:16)). • The Messiah will bring salvation to all the world ([Isa 2:1–5](https://ref.ly/Isa2:1-Isa2:5)). Members of other nations will even serve as priests and Levites ([66:20–21](https://ref.ly/Isa66:20-Isa66:21)), but only if they repent and believe in the true God.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 11:10

## Isaiah 11:11 (id: 15840)

The first time the Lord reached out his hand was at the Exodus; the second time will occur in the future when the Messiah gathers Gentiles ([11:10](https://ref.ly/Isa11:10)) and his own people who live among the Gentile nations ([11:11](https://ref.ly/Isa11:11)). The Exodus remained the paradigm of hope for those in exile (see [11:16](https://ref.ly/Isa11:16); [35:8](https://ref.ly/Isa35:8); [40:3](https://ref.ly/Isa40:3); [43:19–20](https://ref.ly/Isa43:19-Isa43:20); [48:21](https://ref.ly/Isa48:21)). • The remnant of his people included Israelites who survived the Assyrian and Babylonian invasions and were sent into exile.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 11:11

## Isaiah 11:13 (id: 15841)

jealousy between Israel and Judah: The two kingdoms had become enemies during Judah’s war with the alliance of Syria and Israel (see [7:1–12](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa7:12)). In the future, they would cooperate in joint military expeditions.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 11:13

## Isaiah 11:14 (id: 15842)

Philistia to the west: The Philistines were Israel’s historic enemy. • The nations to the east included the Edomites, Moabites, and Ammonites. All foes would be vanquished.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 11:14

## Isaiah 11:15–16 (id: 15843)

Using imagery from Israel’s past exodus, Isaiah encouraged his listeners by promising that God would bring his people out of Assyria the way he brought them out of Egypt ([Exod 14:29–15:18](https://ref.ly/Exod14:29-Exod15:18); see also [Rev 16:12](https://ref.ly/Rev16:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 11:15–ISA 11:16

## Isaiah 12:1 (id: 15852)

God’s comfort is the main subject of chs [40–66](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa66:24).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 12:1

## Isaiah 12:1–6 (id: 15851)

The prophet’s personal song of praise for God’s salvation closes the meditations on trust in chs [7–12](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa12:6). God will prove himself trustworthy in his promises to rescue his people even though they initially refused to trust him. The song anticipates chs [40–66](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa66:24) with its themes of salvation, forgiveness, praise, joy, and the nations.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 12:1–ISA 12:6

## Isaiah 12:2 (id: 15853)

God’s promise to save includes reconciliation ([1:18](https://ref.ly/Isa1:18)), participation ([11:10](https://ref.ly/Isa11:10)), cleansing and protection ([4:3–6](https://ref.ly/Isa4:3-Isa4:6)), peace and confidence ([32:16–17](https://ref.ly/Isa32:16-Isa32:17)), and restoration ([11:6–9](https://ref.ly/Isa11:6-Isa11:9)). • Lord God (Hebrew Yah Yahweh): The first word is an abbreviation of the second. • The Lord God is my strength and my song: See [Exod 15:2](https://ref.ly/Exod15:2); [Ps 118:14](https://ref.ly/Ps118:14).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 12:2

## Isaiah 12:3 (id: 15854)

God’s salvation is like a deep and unending source of water that provides life (see also [41:18](https://ref.ly/Isa41:18); [Ps 87:7](https://ref.ly/Ps87:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 12:3

## Isaiah 12:4–5 (id: 15855)

Tell the nations what he has done: The story of salvation must be told, even beyond the community of Israel ([Pss 67:2](https://ref.ly/Ps67:2); [70:4](https://ref.ly/Ps70:4); [98:2](https://ref.ly/Ps98:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 12:4–ISA 12:5

## Isaiah 12:6 (id: 15856)

See [Pss 48:1](https://ref.ly/Ps48:1); [96:4](https://ref.ly/Ps96:4); [99:2](https://ref.ly/Ps99:2).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 12:6

## Isaiah 13:1 (id: 15865)

At the time of this prophecy, Assyria was the major power. Isaiah anticipated the rise of Babylon as an even crueler kingdom that would destroy Judah and Jerusalem ([39:6–7](https://ref.ly/Isa39:6-Isa39:7); chs [46–47](https://ref.ly/Isa46:1-Isa47:15)). The universal language of [13:1–14:23](https://ref.ly/Isa13:1-Isa14:23) suggests that in this context, Babylon represents all wicked and arrogant nations in the same way that “Babylon the Great” does in the book of Revelation ([Rev 18](https://ref.ly/Rev18:1-Rev18:24)). Therefore, while this description of Babylon’s fall applies to the fall of historic Babylon in 539 BC, it also applies until the final judgment against the ultimate kingdom of evil ([Rev 19](https://ref.ly/Rev19:1-Rev19:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:1

## Isaiah 13:1–23.18 (id: 15857)

This section contains prophecies of judgment against the nations. By including a prophecy against Judah and Jerusalem ([22:1–25](https://ref.ly/Isa22:1-Isa22:25)) in the midst of prophecies against the pagan nations, Isaiah emphasized that Israel’s identity as God’s people would not protect them from God’s punishment when they sinned as the other nations do.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:1

## Isaiah 13:2 (id: 15867)

A signal flag or banner that could be seen clearly from a great distance was often placed on a bare hilltop as a call to battle. • God stands against Babylon as the symbolic representative of all evil kingdoms.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:2

## Isaiah 13:5 (id: 15868)

Just as Assyria was the rod of his anger ([9:4](https://ref.ly/Isa9:4); [10:5](https://ref.ly/Isa10:5), [15](https://ref.ly/Isa10:15)), other nations serve as the Lord’s weapons and carry out his will. The Medes and Persians conquered Babylon in 539 BC. • God’s wrath is an expression of his justice as well as his intent to restore order to the world by obliterating evildoers ([10:25–26](https://ref.ly/Isa10:25-Isa10:26); see [Lam 2:22](https://ref.ly/Lam2:22); [Ezek 7:19](https://ref.ly/Ezek7:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:5

## Isaiah 13:6 (id: 15869)

When pronouncing judgment that is still in the future, the prophets sometimes spoke as though it had already arrived. This emphasized the urgency of turning back to God ([Ezek 30:3](https://ref.ly/Ezek30:3); [Joel 1:15](https://ref.ly/Joel1:15); [3:14](https://ref.ly/Joel3:14); [Obad 1:15](https://ref.ly/Obad1:15); [Zeph 1:7](https://ref.ly/Zeph1:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:6

## Isaiah 13:8 (id: 15870)

Pangs . . . like those of a woman in labor: The day of the Lord brings sudden and overpowering pain and fear for the wicked (see also [Jer 30:6](https://ref.ly/Jer30:6); [Hos 13:13](https://ref.ly/Hos13:13); [Mic 4:9](https://ref.ly/Mic4:9), [10](https://ref.ly/Mic4:10); [5:3](https://ref.ly/Mic5:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:8

## Isaiah 13:10 (id: 15858)

Cosmic upheaval and darkness are common images for the day of the Lord, a time of God’s judgment (see also [Ezek 32:7–8](https://ref.ly/Ezek32:7-Ezek32:8); [Joel 2:10](https://ref.ly/Joel2:10), [31](https://ref.ly/Joel2:31); [3:15](https://ref.ly/Joel3:15); [Matt 24:29](https://ref.ly/Matt24:29); [Mark 13:24](https://ref.ly/Mark13:24); [Rev 6:12–13](https://ref.ly/Rev6:12-Rev6:13); [8:12](https://ref.ly/Rev8:12); cp. [Isa 30:26](https://ref.ly/Isa30:26)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:10

## Isaiah 13:11 (id: 15859)

punish the world: See [Rev 20:11](https://ref.ly/Rev20:11).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:11

## Isaiah 13:12 (id: 15860)

Solomon’s imported gold came from Ophir ([1 Kgs 9:28](https://ref.ly/1Kgs9:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:12

## Isaiah 13:13 (id: 15861)

God will shake the heavens. The earth will move: Cosmic upheaval will accompany God’s coming in judgment (see study note on 5:25; see also [Hag 2:7](https://ref.ly/Hag2:7), [21](https://ref.ly/Hag2:21); [Heb 12:26–27](https://ref.ly/Heb12:26-Heb12:27); [Rev 16:17–18](https://ref.ly/Rev16:17-Rev16:18); [18:21–22](https://ref.ly/Rev18:21-Rev18:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:13
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 5:25 (ID: `16462@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 13:16 (id: 15862)

These terrible acts of war would be perpetrated against Babylon, itself a cruel conquering power (see also [Ps 137:8–9](https://ref.ly/Ps137:8-Ps137:9); [Jer 6:11–12](https://ref.ly/Jer6:11-Jer6:12); [Nah 3:10](https://ref.ly/Nah3:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:16

## Isaiah 13:17 (id: 15863)

The Lord is sovereign and controls history. He can stir up kingdoms to execute his will. • The Medes from northwestern Iran joined Persian King Cyrus in fighting against Babylon in 539 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:17

## Isaiah 13:19–22 (id: 15864)

This picture of Babylon as a perpetually haunted ruin contrasts with its magnificence at its peak (see [Dan 4:29–30](https://ref.ly/Dan4:29-Dan4:30)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:19–ISA 13:22

## Isaiah 13:20 (id: 15866)

Babylon will never be inhabited again: Babylon, often a symbol of idolatry, immorality, imperialism, and oppression in Scripture, will one day come completely to an end (see [Rev 18:2](https://ref.ly/Rev18:2); [19:2](https://ref.ly/Rev19:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 13:20

## Isaiah 14:1 (id: 15879)

The Hebrew word here translated mercy draws from the imagery of a woman’s maternal care for her child to illustrate God’s merciful love for his people (see also [49:15](https://ref.ly/Isa49:15); [66:13](https://ref.ly/Isa66:13)). • God had initially chosen Israel as his special people when he called Abraham (see [41:8](https://ref.ly/Isa41:8); [Gen 12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)). Although their status did not change during the Exile, they faced God’s wrath like any other wicked nation because they had rejected him. • settle once again in their own land: This promise began to be fulfilled in 538 BC (see [Ezra 1:1–11](https://ref.ly/Ezra1:1-Ezra1:11)). • People from many different nations would join Israel as the people of God (see also [Isa 2:3–4](https://ref.ly/Isa2:3-Isa2:4); [11:11–12](https://ref.ly/Isa11:11-Isa11:12); [19:18–25](https://ref.ly/Isa19:18-Isa19:25); [60:1–14](https://ref.ly/Isa60:1-Isa60:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:1

## Isaiah 14:1–2 (id: 15871)

This message for Israel separates two prophecies of judgment against Babylon (ch [13](https://ref.ly/Isa13:1-Isa13:22); [14:3–23](https://ref.ly/Isa14:3-Isa14:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:1–ISA 14:2

## Isaiah 14:2 (id: 15887)

Just as Israel was subject to the Lord, so the nations of the world would submit themselves to the Lord through Israel ([45:14](https://ref.ly/Isa45:14); [49:7](https://ref.ly/Isa49:7), [23](https://ref.ly/Isa49:23); [60:12](https://ref.ly/Isa60:12), [14](https://ref.ly/Isa60:14); [66:23](https://ref.ly/Isa66:23)). • The oppressed nation of Israel will rule over its enemies, assuming a position of power and favor with God.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:2

## Isaiah 14:3 (id: 15891)

God gives . . . rest (i.e., *relief;* [28:12](https://ref.ly/Isa28:12); see [Deut 25:19](https://ref.ly/Deut25:19); [2 Sam 7:11](https://ref.ly/2Sam7:11)) from the sorrow and fear, . . . slavery and chains Israel experienced under foreign oppressors (see [Isa 9:4](https://ref.ly/Isa9:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:3

## Isaiah 14:3–23 (id: 15888)

This taunting song for the king of Babylon is in the form of a funeral dirge (cp. [Rev 18](https://ref.ly/Rev18:1-Rev18:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:3–ISA 14:23

## Isaiah 14:4 (id: 15892)

A taunt is a mocking comparison in song form. In this instance, the king of Babylon is compared to a dead man entering the world of the dead.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:4

## Isaiah 14:7–8 (id: 15893)

The land and people will be at rest and quiet because the oppression has ended and the king of Babylon has died. • The whole creation will join in praise, able to sing again (see also [42:11](https://ref.ly/Isa42:11); [44:23](https://ref.ly/Isa44:23); cp. [Rom 8:22](https://ref.ly/Rom8:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:7–ISA 14:8

## Isaiah 14:9 (id: 15894)

The Babylonians saw the place of the dead (Hebrew *Sheol*) as a place of no return. • stand up (literally *get up from their thrones*): The thrones reflect the Babylonian concept of the life hereafter as a continuation of the same mode of existence as the present life. It appears that the other kings are honoring the great king of Babylon, but the next verses tell a different story.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:9

## Isaiah 14:10 (id: 15872)

weak as we are: The Babylonian king had no power over anyone after death and was unable to leave Sheol. The Israelites will mock this great king who on earth appeared to have no weaknesses.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:10

## Isaiah 14:11 (id: 15873)

Babylon’s might and power and the sound of the harp were ended, and its magnificence was destroyed (see also [Rev 18:22](https://ref.ly/Rev18:22)). • Maggots and worms symbolized death and decomposition ([Isa 66:24](https://ref.ly/Isa66:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:11

## Isaiah 14:12 (id: 15874)

fallen from heaven, O shining star: These words allude to the Canaanite story of the god Helel’s rebellion against the god El (chief deity of the Canaanite pantheon) and his fall from heaven. Some see the fall of the king of Babylon here as symbolizing the fall of Satan (see [Ezek 28](https://ref.ly/Ezek28:1-Ezek28:26); [Luke 10:18](https://ref.ly/Luke10:18); [Rev 12:9](https://ref.ly/Rev12:9)). However, there is little to suggest that Isaiah understood it in that way. He was thinking of the historical king of Babylon. • son of the morning: The battle took place under the early morning sun. The Latin Vulgate translates the term as *Lucifer* (*morning star*), a name for Satan in Christian tradition, but the Hebrew text makes no apparent reference here to Satan.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:12

## Isaiah 14:13 (id: 15875)

This verse alludes to the Canaanite belief that the chief god El and the other gods were enthroned on Mount Zaphon, a northern mountain (see [Ps 48:2](https://ref.ly/Ps48:2); for a New Testament application, see [Matt 11:23](https://ref.ly/Matt11:23); [Luke 10:15](https://ref.ly/Luke10:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:13

## Isaiah 14:14 (id: 15876)

Most High: See [Gen 14:19–22](https://ref.ly/Gen14:19-Gen14:22).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:14

## Isaiah 14:15–17 (id: 15877)

This is a restatement of [14:9–11](https://ref.ly/Isa14:9-Isa14:11). The dead spirits inhabiting the place of the dead will be startled and amazed that the Babylonian king, who ruled the world with his merciless might, has absolutely no power in death.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:15–ISA 14:17

## Isaiah 14:18–20 (id: 15878)

The absence of a proper burial was a sign of great shame and dishonor. Unlike other kings, the king of Babylon would be disgraced in judgment.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:18–ISA 14:20

## Isaiah 14:21 (id: 15880)

As another sign of disgrace, the king of Babylon would have no children to provide a future legacy. • because of their father’s sins: God looks at individuals in relationship to their families and their people. Here, the Babylonian king’s children had joint responsibility for their father’s actions (see also [Deut 5:9–10](https://ref.ly/Deut5:9-Deut5:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:21

## Isaiah 14:22–23 (id: 15881)

Isaiah summarized the previous taunt ([14:3–21](https://ref.ly/Isa14:3-Isa14:21)) with this prophecy, spoken in the first person. God decrees the destruction and desolation of Babylon.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:22–ISA 14:23

## Isaiah 14:24 (id: 15883)

I have planned . . . I have decided: No nation can either diminish or resist God’s plans to bring judgment against Assyria or his plans in general. The prophets understood and communicated God’s plan so that his people could respond appropriately.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:24

## Isaiah 14:24–27 (id: 15882)

This prophecy resumes declaring judgment on Assyria (see ch [10](https://ref.ly/Isa10:1-Isa10:34)). The placement of this prophecy after the judgment against Babylon suggests close connections between Assyria and Babylon.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:24–ISA 14:27

## Isaiah 14:25 (id: 15884)

This prophecy told what would happen when Sennacherib attacked Hezekiah some years later in 701 BC (see chs [36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa37:38)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:25

## Isaiah 14:26 (id: 15885)

The Lord’s power over Assyria is just one example of his sovereignty over the whole earth.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:26

## Isaiah 14:29 (id: 15886)

The king who attacked Philistia was probably Ahaz ([14:28](https://ref.ly/Isa14:28)). • a more poisonous snake: This probably refers to one of the later Assyrian kings, either Sennacherib (701 BC), Esarhaddon (680 BC), or Ashurbanipal (668 BC).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:29

## Isaiah 14:31 (id: 15889)

The advancing army, probably Assyria, would stir up clouds of dust like smoke that were ominously visible in the north.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:31

## Isaiah 14:32 (id: 15890)

The Lord has built Jerusalem: God was fully willing and capable of defending Judah, and he did not want the kingdom to make alliances with nations such as the Philistines to protect themselves from the invading Assyrians.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 14:32

## Isaiah 15:1 (id: 15896)

Moab was Judah’s enemy, situated on the eastern side of the Dead Sea. • In one night: Destruction would be sudden and decisive.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:1

## Isaiah 15:1–16.14 (id: 15895)

This message of judgment and lament concerned Moab’s fall. The extensive references to Moabite place names indicate that Isaiah was thoroughly familiar with Moab.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:1–ISA 16:14

## Isaiah 15:2 (id: 15897)

In the ancient Near East, actions such as shaving or cutting off their beards revealed deep sorrow (see [Jer 48:37](https://ref.ly/Jer48:37)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:2

## Isaiah 15:4 (id: 15898)

Heshbon was the former capital of Sihon ([Num 21:23–26](https://ref.ly/Num21:23-Num21:26)). • Elealeh was located close to Heshbon. • Jahaz was between Heshbon and the Arnon River (see [Jer 48:34](https://ref.ly/Jer48:34)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:4

## Isaiah 15:5 (id: 15899)

My heart weeps for Moab: The prophet joined in the lament to express the greatness of Moab’s pain. • Zoar was in the southeastern area of the Dead Sea ([Gen 14:2](https://ref.ly/Gen14:2); [19:23](https://ref.ly/Gen19:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:5

## Isaiah 15:6 (id: 15900)

Identification of the waters of Nimrim is uncertain (see also [Jer 48:34](https://ref.ly/Jer48:34)). • dried up . . . scorched . . . tender plants: For the theme of natural resources drying up as a form of judgment, see study note on Isa 24:4.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:6
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 24:4 (ID: `16042@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 15:7–8 (id: 15901)

The precise locations of the Ravine of Willows and of Eglaim and Beer\-elim are uncertain.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:7–ISA 15:8

## Isaiah 15:9 (id: 15902)

* *Dimon* is a play on the Hebrew word for *blood* (*dam*).
* *blood*: Many people from Moab had already died, but their punishment had just started.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 15:9

## Isaiah 16:1 (id: 15906)

Moab was known for its sheep (see [2 Kgs 3:4](https://ref.ly/2Kgs3:4)). The lambs were sent as tribute to Judah in recognition of Judah’s sovereignty over Moab. • Sela (*the cliff*), an unknown remote site, was apparently where the Moabites escaped through the desert.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:1

## Isaiah 16:1–4 (id: 15903)

Moab is making a request to Judah for asylum (protection in another country). 

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:1–ISA 16:4

## Isaiah 16:3–4 (id: 15907)

Do not betray us . . . Hide them: Moab’s situation would become so dire that they would beg for asylum in other lands.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:3–ISA 16:4

## Isaiah 16:4–5 (id: 15908)

This prophecy of salvation spoke of the future of David’s dynasty. • Activities such as oppression and destruction would cease with the end of Moabite hostilities (see [2 Kgs 13:20](https://ref.ly/2Kgs13:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:4–ISA 16:5

## Isaiah 16:5 (id: 15909)

God would establish one of David’s descendants as king (see also [9:7](https://ref.ly/Isa9:7); [11:1–5](https://ref.ly/Isa11:1-Isa11:5), [10–12](https://ref.ly/Isa11:10-Isa11:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:5

## Isaiah 16:6 (id: 15911)

The Moabites’ hardened state of pride and arrogance and rage and boasting prevented them from humbling themselves before God.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:6

## Isaiah 16:6–11 (id: 15910)

The response to Moab’s request for asylum ([16:1–4](https://ref.ly/Isa16:1-Isa16:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:6–ISA 16:11

## Isaiah 16:8 (id: 15912)

The vineyards at Sibmah were located by the Moabite city of Heshbon. • Moab was like a luxuriant vine that extends far and wide, but Israel’s beautiful vine reached to the ends of the earth ([27:6](https://ref.ly/Isa27:6)). • The location of Jazer is unknown.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:8

## Isaiah 16:12 (id: 15904)

The hilltops were sites for pagan shrines (see [15:2](https://ref.ly/Isa15:2)). • No one will be able to save them, including the idols the Moabites called on for protection (see [40:18–19](https://ref.ly/Isa40:18-Isa40:19); [44:18–19](https://ref.ly/Isa44:18-Isa44:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:12

## Isaiah 16:14 (id: 15905)

Within three years, counting each day (literally *Within three years, as a servant bound by contract would count them*; see also [21:16](https://ref.ly/Isa21:16)): A man who had sold himself into servitude for a period of time would calculate how much time remained right down to the final day. This verse refers to events that are unknown. • The glory of Moab will be ended*,* probably because of the Assyrian conquest of Moab (late 700s BC). • only a feeble few will be left alive: Though decimated by war, the Moabites still existed during Nehemiah’s time ([Neh 13:23](https://ref.ly/Neh13:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 16:14

## Isaiah 17:1 (id: 15919)

Damascus, the ringleader in the alliance of Syria and Israel against Judah, fell when the Assyrian king Tiglath\-pileser III invaded in 732 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:1

## Isaiah 17:1–20.6 (id: 15913)

This section contains prophecies of judgment against Damascus, Israel, Ethiopia, and Egypt. The scene moves from the crisis of 734 BC (the alliance of Syria and Israel—see study note on 7:1–25) to the Assyrian crisis in 701 BC (see chs [36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa37:38)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:1–ISA 20:6
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 7:1-25 (ID: `16720@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 17:2 (id: 15920)

The towns of Aroer were east of the Dead Sea (see [2 Kgs 10:32–33](https://ref.ly/2Kgs10:32-2Kgs10:33)). • Flocks will graze in the streets: The city would be useless for any other purpose.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:2

## Isaiah 17:3 (id: 15921)

The strategic fortified towns of Israel were the pride of Israel, but they could not resist God’s purposes.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:3

## Isaiah 17:4 (id: 15923)

Israel’s glory will grow dim: God will judge all human pride. • Israel’s robust body will waste away like a person who is ill.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:4

## Isaiah 17:4–11 (id: 15922)

The vision shifts from Damascus ([17:1–3](https://ref.ly/Isa17:1-Isa17:3)) to the northern kingdom of Israel; Damascus and Israel were allies in the war against Judah, and both were later destroyed by Assyria.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:4–ISA 17:11

## Isaiah 17:5 (id: 15924)

The valley of Rephaim was a fertile valley in Judah; however, it looked bare after each harvest.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:5

## Isaiah 17:6 (id: 15925)

Although the God of Israel judged the wickedness of Israel, he was still its God.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:6

## Isaiah 17:7 (id: 15926)

The people referred to here are either Israelites or humanity at large. • look . . . turn their eyes: They will again put their trust in the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:7

## Isaiah 17:8 (id: 15927)

Canaanites and then Israelites worshiped the fertility goddess Asherah in wooded areas or, in the absence of wooded areas, using Asherah poles (see [27:9](https://ref.ly/Isa27:9); [65:3](https://ref.ly/Isa65:3); [Mic 5:14](https://ref.ly/Mic5:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:8

## Isaiah 17:9 (id: 15928)

The Hivites and Amorites were members of the original population of Canaan; they were to be destroyed when the Israelites conquered the land (see [Deut 20:17](https://ref.ly/Deut20:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:9

## Isaiah 17:10 (id: 15914)

The Israelites had mixed faith in the Lord with elements of Baal worship. They turned from the Lord, who alone is God and will not be held alongside an idol in people’s hearts. • The term Rock refers to the Lord’s ability to provide refuge (see [26:4](https://ref.ly/Isa26:4); [30:29](https://ref.ly/Isa30:29); [Ps 18:2](https://ref.ly/Ps18:2), [46](https://ref.ly/Ps18:46)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:10

## Isaiah 17:12 (id: 15916)

At times, the sea represents opposition to God (see [Job 7:12](https://ref.ly/Job7:12); [26:12](https://ref.ly/Job26:12); [Pss 46:3](https://ref.ly/Ps46:3); [93:3](https://ref.ly/Ps93:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:12

## Isaiah 17:12–14 (id: 15915)

God was present with Judah during the crisis.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:12–ISA 17:14

## Isaiah 17:13 (id: 15917)

God will silence them: The Lord is sovereign over the nations, as he is over the sea ([Ps 65:7](https://ref.ly/Ps65:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:13

## Isaiah 17:14 (id: 15918)

In the evening . . . by dawn: Despite frightening circumstances, Israel can have confidence in the Lord’s ability to save quickly (see [37:36–38](https://ref.ly/Isa37:36-Isa37:38)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 17:14

## Isaiah 18:1 (id: 15929)

Ethiopia was at the southern extremity of the kingdom of Egypt. At the time, Egypt was ruled by the dynasty of the Ethiopian Piankhi.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 18:1

## Isaiah 18:3 (id: 15930)

Both the battle flag and ram’s horn were used to marshal and send signals to armies in battle (see [Judg 3:27](https://ref.ly/Judg3:27); [6:34](https://ref.ly/Judg6:34); [2 Sam 2:28](https://ref.ly/2Sam2:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 18:3

## Isaiah 18:4 (id: 15931)

The Lord is untroubled by human activities; he watches quietly from his dwelling place and acts in his own time (see [Ps 2:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ps2:1-Ps2:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 18:4

## Isaiah 18:5 (id: 15932)

your plans are ripening: Ethiopia had made every effort to form alliances to protect itself against Assyria, but its alliance with Judah would not help.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 18:5

## Isaiah 18:7 (id: 15933)

Conquered or weaker nations were often forced to pay tribute to a dominant king in the region (see [16:1](https://ref.ly/Isa16:1); [2 Kgs 3:4–5](https://ref.ly/2Kgs3:4-2Kgs3:5)). The Ethiopians would come bringing gifts to honor the Lord for his defeat of the Assyrians (see also [2 Chr 32:23](https://ref.ly/2Chr32:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 18:7

## Isaiah 19:1 (id: 15945)

Riding on a swift cloud shows God’s sovereignty and power over creation ([Ps 104:3](https://ref.ly/Ps104:3)). He is coming in judgment against Judah’s enemies (see [Deut 33:26](https://ref.ly/Deut33:26); [Pss 18:9–10](https://ref.ly/Ps18:9-Ps18:10); [68:4](https://ref.ly/Ps68:4); [97:2–3](https://ref.ly/Ps97:2-Ps97:3)). • The idols of Egypt tremble because they are weak and impotent, unable to help (see [Isa 40:18](https://ref.ly/Isa40:18); [45:16](https://ref.ly/Isa45:16); [46:1](https://ref.ly/Isa46:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:1

## Isaiah 19:1–10 (id: 15934)

Egypt was vulnerable, depending on the Nile for food, fish, and industry. When the Lord struck the Nile, Egypt would be in dire straits, and their gods would not be able to help.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:1–ISA 19:10

## Isaiah 19:1–20.6 (id: 15935)

See also [Jer 46](https://ref.ly/Jer46:1-Jer46:28); [Ezek 29–32](https://ref.ly/Ezek29:1-Ezek32:32).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:1–ISA 20:6

## Isaiah 19:2 (id: 15951)

Egypt had a history of civil wars that ravaged the country. Egypt’s fragile unity was breaking apart.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:2

## Isaiah 19:3 (id: 15952)

Idols . . . spirits, mediums, and those who consult the spirits of the dead were called upon in an attempt to ward off impending destruction (see [8:19–22](https://ref.ly/Isa8:19-Isa8:22); [47:12–13](https://ref.ly/Isa47:12-Isa47:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:3

## Isaiah 19:4 (id: 15953)

hard, cruel master: In 671 BC, the Assyrian king Esarhaddon invaded Egypt and destroyed the royal city of Memphis. In 663 BC, Ashurbanipal attacked and destroyed Thebes (see [Nah 3:8–10](https://ref.ly/Nah3:8-Nah3:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:4

## Isaiah 19:5 (id: 15954)

The waters of the Nile were Egypt’s lifeline. Any disturbance in the regular pattern of flooding affected the whole nation. In ancient Egypt, the Nile was considered to be a god. However, the true God could easily dry it up.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:5

## Isaiah 19:9 (id: 15955)

There was no flax or thread: The drought crippled the linen industry.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:9

## Isaiah 19:11 (id: 15937)

Zoan was the capital of Egypt, located in the Nile delta (see [Num 13:22](https://ref.ly/Num13:22)). • officials . . . best counsel: The king depended on the strategies and counsel of wise men ([Isa 19:12](https://ref.ly/Isa19:12); see [Gen 41:39–40](https://ref.ly/Gen41:39-Gen41:40)). • The officials of Zoan were known for all their wise ancestors*,* and Egypt liked to brag about them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:11

## Isaiah 19:11–15 (id: 15936)

The prophet taunted the leaders and wise men of Egypt, who were helpless to avert tragedy.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:11–ISA 19:15

## Isaiah 19:12 (id: 15938)

Where are your wise counselors: Cp. [1 Cor 1:20](https://ref.ly/1Cor1:20).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:12

## Isaiah 19:13 (id: 15939)

Memphis was an ancient capital in Egypt located south of the Nile delta. It was destroyed by Esarhaddon in 671 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:13

## Isaiah 19:15 (id: 15940)

There is nothing Egypt can do: No leader, idol, or conventional wisdom can stand against God’s judgment. • The head and the tail represented all of Egypt’s leaders from the greatest to the least important (see [9:14–15](https://ref.ly/Isa9:14-Isa9:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:15

## Isaiah 19:16 (id: 15941)

An upraised fist (literally *waving the hand*) was a threatening motion.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:16

## Isaiah 19:18 (id: 15942)

five of Egypt’s cities: This is probably just a way of saying “a number of cities” (see also [17:6](https://ref.ly/Isa17:6); [30:17](https://ref.ly/Isa30:17)). • Jews living in Egypt after the destruction of Jerusalem spoke Hebrew, here called the language of Canaan. • Heliopolis was devoted to Re, the sun god. At a time when Judah was resisting the Lord, Isaiah envisioned the Egyptians following the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:18

## Isaiah 19:19 (id: 15944)

This altar or monument refers either to the temple that was built by Jews at Elephantine in Egypt (500 BC), to some other Jewish place of worship, or perhaps to a memorial the Egyptians constructed in devotion to the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:19

## Isaiah 19:19–25 (id: 15943)

The new Egypt would receive a place in God’s purposes together with Assyria and Israel.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:19–ISA 19:25

## Isaiah 19:20 (id: 15946)

The altar ([19:19](https://ref.ly/Isa19:19)) would be a sign and a witness of the Egyptians’ religious connection with Israel (see [19:24–25](https://ref.ly/Isa19:24-Isa19:25)). • God answers the prayers of those who cry to the Lord for help. • The Lord is a savior; he would protect the Egyptians even as he protected Jerusalem from the Assyrians.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:20

## Isaiah 19:22 (id: 15947)

strike Egypt: The Hebrew verb used here is the same as the one used for the tenth plague ([Exod 12:13](https://ref.ly/Exod12:13), [23](https://ref.ly/Exod12:23), [27](https://ref.ly/Exod12:27)), bringing up associations of what God did during the Exodus. • The Lord, who brings a plague, can also bring healing. • he will listen to their pleas and heal them: This expresses a covenantal relationship between the Lord and Egypt (see [Isa 65:24](https://ref.ly/Isa65:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:22

## Isaiah 19:23 (id: 15948)

Traditionally, Egypt and Assyria were enemies of one another and of Israel. They were idolatrous to the core, but they would leave behind their enmity and their idols to worship the true God. • In the rugged terrain of the ancient Near East, a highway provided a means for safe, easy travel and trade (see [11:16](https://ref.ly/Isa11:16); [35:8](https://ref.ly/Isa35:8); [40:3](https://ref.ly/Isa40:3); [62:10](https://ref.ly/Isa62:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:23

## Isaiah 19:24 (id: 15949)

Israel will be . . . a blessing in fulfillment of God’s promise to Abraham ([Gen 12:3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:24

## Isaiah 19:25 (id: 15950)

Two designations usually reserved for Israel, my people and the land I have made (see [29:22](https://ref.ly/Isa29:22); [32:18](https://ref.ly/Isa32:18)), are applied here to foreign nations. The Lord promised to regard those from the nations who turn to him as his special possession ([63:17](https://ref.ly/Isa63:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 19:25

## Isaiah 20:1 (id: 15975)

Sargon of Assyria is Sargon II (721–705 BC). • Ashdod was one of the five Philistine cities that rebelled against Assyria in 713 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 20:1

## Isaiah 20:1–5 (id: 15974)

Ashdod’s reliance on Egypt and Ethiopia was useless. As a complement to his words, Isaiah walked about exposed and barefoot, a symbolic action for what God would do.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 20:1–ISA 20:5

## Isaiah 20:2 (id: 15976)

Prophets characteristically wore burlap or other rough clothing as a sign of mourning over the sins of the people (see [2 Kgs 1:8](https://ref.ly/2Kgs1:8); [Mark 1:6](https://ref.ly/Mark1:6)). • naked: That is, exposed in some way, possibly even completely nude.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 20:2

## Isaiah 20:3–4 (id: 15977)

This symbolic event took place some three years before the fall of Ashdod (see [20:2](https://ref.ly/Isa20:2)). Through it, Isaiah became an object lesson for the people (see [8:18](https://ref.ly/Isa8:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 20:3–ISA 20:4

## Isaiah 20:5–6 (id: 15978)

Philistia’s downfall was a warning to the people of Judah, who also relied on Egypt and other alliances rather than on God.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 20:5–ISA 20:6

## Isaiah 21:1 (id: 15989)

concerning Babylon—the desert by the sea: This description of Babylon was perhaps an ironic way to say that the land was physically lush but spiritually desolate. • Disaster . . . from the desert: Literally *from the desert, from the terrifying land* (cp. [Deut 8:15](https://ref.ly/Deut8:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:1

## Isaiah 21:2 (id: 15990)

I see: The prophet personalized Babylon’s experience, which intensifies his poetry, as does the terse language and the repetition of similar sounds, such as the betrayer betraying (Hebrew habboged boged) and the destroyer destroying (Hebrew hashoded shoded; also in [33:1](https://ref.ly/Isa33:1)). • The Elamites lived to the northeast of Babylon. Along with the Medes, they were archrivals of the Babylonians. • I will make an end to all the groaning Babylon caused: Babylon had caused groaning by attacking and enslaving many nations. It is likely that the Elamites and Medes played a part in the overthrow of Babylon in 539 BC ([21:9](https://ref.ly/Isa21:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:2

## Isaiah 21:3 (id: 15991)

I grow faint . . . I am too afraid: Through his vision, Isaiah experienced the terror of the Babylonian people (see also [Dan 8:27](https://ref.ly/Dan8:27); [10:16–17](https://ref.ly/Dan10:16-Dan10:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:3

## Isaiah 21:7 (id: 15992)

The riders were messengers who reported what had taken place on the battlefield.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:7

## Isaiah 21:9 (id: 15993)

Babylon is fallen, fallen: Assyrian king Sennacherib destroyed the city of Babylon in 689 BC. The final fall of Babylon was in 539 BC. In Scripture, Babylon represents all the ungodly power structures of this world, including nations and kingdoms that do not submit to God and his word. The apostle John gave hope with his prophecy that all Babylons will fall ([Rev 14:8](https://ref.ly/Rev14:8); [18:2](https://ref.ly/Rev18:2)). • All the idols of Babylon lie broken: False gods could not save the Babylonians (see [Isa 46:1–2](https://ref.ly/Isa46:1-Isa46:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:9

## Isaiah 21:10 (id: 15979)

O my people: The focus shifted back to the people of Judah. • Judah would be oppressed (threshed and winnowed) by the Babylonians but Babylon, too, would fall. • I have told you everything: The prophet had been faithful in his duty to report what the Lord revealed to him.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:10

## Isaiah 21:11–12 (id: 15980)

Edom: Hebrew *Seir*, which was the mountainous area of Edom. • how much longer until morning: Edom’s suffering would be a long ordeal. The Assyrians were dominant in the ancient Near East during the 700s and 600s BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:11–ISA 21:12

## Isaiah 21:11–17 (id: 15981)

These two prophecies pertain to Edom and Arabia, located in the territory between Babylon and Israel. These outlying regions suffered under Assyrian domination, and like Judah, they would not be able to rest under Babylon’s oppressive regime.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:11–ISA 21:17

## Isaiah 21:12 (id: 15982)

Morning is coming*,* but it would mark the beginning of another oppressive era. Because the Babylonians were on the horizon, night will soon return.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:12

## Isaiah 21:13 (id: 15984)

Dedan was a tribe in Arabia (see [Ezek 27:20](https://ref.ly/Ezek27:20); [38:13](https://ref.ly/Ezek38:13)). • Caravans would hide in the deserts of Arabia from attacks by the Assyrians and Babylonians, both of whom harassed the Arabian tribes (see [Isa 21:1–10](https://ref.ly/Isa21:1-Isa21:10); [Jer 49:28–29](https://ref.ly/Jer49:28-Jer49:29)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:13

## Isaiah 21:13–17 (id: 15983)

Compare [Jeremiah 49:28–33](https://ref.ly/Jer49:28-Jer49:33).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:13–ISA 21:17

## Isaiah 21:14 (id: 15985)

Tema was an oasis in Arabia on a main trade route from Babylon.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:14

## Isaiah 21:15 (id: 15986)

Assyrian weapons of war such as the drawn sword and the bent bow were highly advanced compared to the primitive weapons used by desert peoples.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:15

## Isaiah 21:16 (id: 15987)

Kedar was an Arabian tribe that had great wealth and possessions (see [60:7](https://ref.ly/Isa60:7); [Ezek 27:21](https://ref.ly/Ezek27:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:16

## Isaiah 21:17 (id: 15988)

Only a few . . . will survive: Cp. [10:22](https://ref.ly/Isa10:22); [14:30](https://ref.ly/Isa14:30); [16:14](https://ref.ly/Isa16:14); [24:6](https://ref.ly/Isa24:6), [13](https://ref.ly/Isa24:13); [37:32](https://ref.ly/Isa37:32).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 21:17

## Isaiah 22:1 (id: 16003)

concerning Jerusalem—the Valley of Vision: The meaning of the Hebrew phrase is uncertain. Assuming that it refers to Jerusalem (see [22:5](https://ref.ly/Isa22:5)), it is ironic: Jerusalem sits atop a hill, and visions were not typically thought to originate in a *valley.* Similarly, Jerusalem was proud of its self\-perceived religious *vision*, but its inhabitants were spiritually blind (see [6:10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:10); [42:18](https://ref.ly/Isa42:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:1

## Isaiah 22:1–4 (id: 15995)

The prophet rebukes the people for their excitement over the Assyrian retreat (701 BC). They did not foresee that the Babylonian army would later (in 586 BC) complete what the Assyrians failed to do, namely, the destruction of Jerusalem.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:1–ISA 22:4

## Isaiah 22:1–25 (id: 15994)

This message addresses the Assyrian siege of Jerusalem in 701 BC (see chs [36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa37:38); [2 Kgs 18:17–19:37](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:17-2Kgs19:37)) and perhaps the people’s preparation for the subsequent Babylonian siege in 588–586 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:1–ISA 22:25

## Isaiah 22:2 (id: 16007)

The reveling city was full of feasting and drinking ([22:13](https://ref.ly/Isa22:13); see [23:7](https://ref.ly/Isa23:7); [32:13](https://ref.ly/Isa32:13)), probably in response to the Assyrian retreat. Now a new threat approaches—the Babylonian siege (586 BC). • The people died not in battle but by famine and disease. Siege warfare left the inhabitants of the city cut off from needed agricultural resources and in a terrible sanitation and health crisis.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:2

## Isaiah 22:3 (id: 16008)

Leaders might try to flee, but they would not escape (see [2 Kgs 25:4–6](https://ref.ly/2Kgs25:4-2Kgs25:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:3

## Isaiah 22:4 (id: 16009)

Leave me alone to weep: Isaiah mourned the victims and the decimation of his people (see [6:13](https://ref.ly/Isa6:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:4

## Isaiah 22:5 (id: 16011)

Jerusalem’s walls were broken by the invading Babylonians, who breached them with battering rams ([2 Kgs 25:4](https://ref.ly/2Kgs25:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:5

## Isaiah 22:5–8 (id: 16010)

The defeat of Judah would be a manifestation of the yet\-future day of the Lord (see study note on 2:5–22). Jerusalem would be impotent in the day of disaster.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:5–ISA 22:8
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 2:5-22 (ID: `15969@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 22:6 (id: 16012)

Elamites: Elam was to the east of Babylon and would later play a role in Babylon’s defeat (see [21:2](https://ref.ly/Isa21:2); also [Jer 49:34–39](https://ref.ly/Jer49:34-Jer49:39)). • The actual location of Kir is unknown (see also [Amos 1:5](https://ref.ly/Amos1:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:6

## Isaiah 22:7 (id: 16013)

The Kidron and Hinnom valleys were located to the east and south of Jerusalem.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:7

## Isaiah 22:8 (id: 16015)

to the armory: This magnificent building was both an armory and a storage place for valuables (see [39:2](https://ref.ly/Isa39:2); [1 Kgs 7:2–6](https://ref.ly/1Kgs7:2-1Kgs7:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:8

## Isaiah 22:8–11 (id: 16014)

The inhabitants of Jerusalem planned for war by counting weapons, checking walls, and preparing a water supply. However, the people did not consider God’s plans (see [13:1–14:23](https://ref.ly/Isa13:1-Isa14:23)). Although Hezekiah was very prominent in these efforts, the second person verbs are plural. Isaiah was apparently not singling out Hezekiah, as he did Hezekiah’s father Ahaz ([7:1–4](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa7:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:8–ISA 22:11

## Isaiah 22:9 (id: 16016)

Jerusalem (literally *the city of David*): Calling Jerusalem “the city of David” evokes memories of Israel’s greatest king and God’s blessing upon him and the nation (see also [60:14](https://ref.ly/Isa60:14)). • You store up water in the lower pool: Hezekiah expended great energy in ensuring a constant supply of water (see[22:11](https://ref.ly/Isa22:11); [2 Kgs 20:20](https://ref.ly/2Kgs20:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:9

## Isaiah 22:12–14 (id: 15996)

Judah’s feasting and disobedience resulted in a prophecy of judgment.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:12–ISA 22:14

## Isaiah 22:14 (id: 15997)

you will never be forgiven for this sin: The people of Judah added sin upon sin to the point that God would justly condemn them. This foreshadowed the Exile; although it took place in 586 BC, Isaiah was already anticipating it in 701 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:14

## Isaiah 22:15 (id: 15999)

The actions of Shebna represent among the populace a lack of confidence in God’s ability to rescue Jerusalem from the Assyrian attack. • A palace administrator held the highest possible position in the royal court (see [22:21–22](https://ref.ly/Isa22:21-Isa22:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:15

## Isaiah 22:15–25 (id: 15998)

Shebna and Eliakim were two royal officials in King Hezekiah’s court (see [2 Kgs 18:18](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:18); [19:2](https://ref.ly/2Kgs19:2)). Apparently assuming that death at the hands of the Assyrians was inevitable, Shebna planned for an ostentatious burial place ([Isa 22:16](https://ref.ly/Isa22:16)). Although the rebuke of Shebna preceded Assyria’s siege of Jerusalem in 701 BC (see [22:19–21](https://ref.ly/Isa22:19-Isa22:21); [36:3](https://ref.ly/Isa36:3)), it connects well with the admonitions of [22:1–13](https://ref.ly/Isa22:1-Isa22:13) regarding the ensuing Babylonian siege.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:15–ISA 22:25

## Isaiah 22:16 (id: 16000)

Shebna was building a beautiful tomb . . . a monument. This lavish burial place was intended to last for centuries.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:16

## Isaiah 22:18 (id: 16001)

toss you away into a distant, barren land: Shebna would not find a resting place or have a memorial (cp. [14:11–20](https://ref.ly/Isa14:11-Isa14:20)). • your glorious chariots: In his high position, Shebna had lived in luxury.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:18

## Isaiah 22:19–20 (id: 16002)

drive you out of office: Shebna ([22:15](https://ref.ly/Isa22:15)) was demoted to court secretary by the time of Jerusalem’s siege, when Eliakim ([22:20](https://ref.ly/Isa22:20)) was the palace administrator ([36:3](https://ref.ly/Isa36:3)). Eliakim was a true servant of the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:19–ISA 22:20

## Isaiah 22:21 (id: 16004)

A leader is like a father to the people when he truly cares for them and addresses their needs. In contrast, Shebna was concerned only for himself.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:21

## Isaiah 22:22 (id: 16005)

The key to the house of David represents a high position of honor in the royal court (see also [Rev 3:7](https://ref.ly/Rev3:7)). • When he opens doors . . . when he closes doors: The officer with the highest position has sole authority in giving access to the king (cp. [Matt 16:19](https://ref.ly/Matt16:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:22

## Isaiah 22:25 (id: 16006)

I will pull out the nail: Even godly Eliakim would not be able to save Judah or David’s dynasty from God’s judgment. The people of Judah would go into exile more than a century later ([39:6–7](https://ref.ly/Isa39:6-Isa39:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 22:25

## Isaiah 23:1 (id: 16024)

The ships of Tarshish plied the sea between the Phoenician coast and the port of Tarshish in Spain (see [2:16](https://ref.ly/Isa2:16); [23:6](https://ref.ly/Isa23:6)). • rumors you heard in Cyprus: Cyprus was the last port of call for sailors coming home from Spain before the final leg of their journey to Tyre.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:1

## Isaiah 23:1–18 (id: 16017)

Tyre was a prosperous seaport northwest of Israel. The relationship between Judah and Tyre went back to the time of David and Solomon ([1 Kgs 5:8–9](https://ref.ly/1Kgs5:8-1Kgs5:9)). Tyre, with its proximity to plentiful forests and to the Mediterranean Sea, was one of the commercial centers of the ancient Near East. Its fleets were renowned, and its colonies brought it great prestige and riches. The city came under repeated attack by the Assyrians, Babylonians, and Macedonians, and it fell to Alexander the Great in 332 BC. Isaiah not only prophesied the end of Tyre but by implication warned all whose wealth and earthly securities lead to pride instead of dependence on God (see [Isa 2:12–16](https://ref.ly/Isa2:12-Isa2:16); [Matt 11:21](https://ref.ly/Matt11:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:1–ISA 23:18

## Isaiah 23:2 (id: 16025)

Sidon was a Phoenician port to the north of Tyre. • Tyre’s commercial network of traders was extensive.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:2

## Isaiah 23:3 (id: 16026)

Fish, agricultural produce, flax, and linen were all harvests from along the Nile (see ch [19](https://ref.ly/Isa19:1-Isa19:25)). • Tyre was known as the marketplace of the world, comparable to modern cities like New York, Hong Kong, or London.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:3

## Isaiah 23:4 (id: 16027)

Part of the great port city of Tyre was on an island. The island city was a fortress of the sea*,* but it fell in 332 BC. • I am childless; I have no sons or daughters: This metaphor was a judgment on Tyre’s colonial ports around the Mediterranean Sea. In the ancient world, to reach old age without having children was considered a terrible fate (see [49:21](https://ref.ly/Isa49:21); [54:1](https://ref.ly/Isa54:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:4

## Isaiah 23:5 (id: 16028)

Egypt depended on the ships from Tyre for the export of its products (see [23:3](https://ref.ly/Isa23:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:5

## Isaiah 23:8 (id: 16029)

The Lord had brought this disaster on Tyre ([23:9](https://ref.ly/Isa23:9)). • Through commercial links, Tyre had created wealth for itself and its trading partners; in this sense, Tyre was a great creator of kingdoms.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:8

## Isaiah 23:10 (id: 16018)

Sweep over the land like the flooding Nile: This difficult phrase means either (1\) that the sailors who had returned from Tarshish were being invited to loot ruined Tyre; or (2\) that the people of Tarshish were forced to move back to their agricultural fields to make their living (instead of trading) now that Tyre had been destroyed.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:10

## Isaiah 23:11 (id: 16019)

Phoenicia was the region north of Israel. Tyre and Sidon were its main cities.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:11

## Isaiah 23:13 (id: 16020)

Babylonia was stripped and looted by the Assyrians under Sennacherib (689 BC); eventually it rose to be a mighty empire once again.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:13

## Isaiah 23:15–18 (id: 16021)

There was hope for Tyre.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:15–ISA 23:18

## Isaiah 23:17 (id: 16022)

Metaphorically, Tyre was a prostitute because of its indifferent willingness to sell anything to anyone (cp. [Rev 17:2](https://ref.ly/Rev17:2); [18:3](https://ref.ly/Rev18:3), [12–13](https://ref.ly/Rev18:12-Rev18:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:17

## Isaiah 23:18 (id: 16023)

Even though Tyre’s earnings came from unrighteous commerce, the profits will be given to the Lord rather than being squandered. • There will be good food and fine clothing for the Lord’s priests. The theme of the wealth of the nations being brought to the Lord in Jerusalem is more fully developed in [60:5–11](https://ref.ly/Isa60:5-Isa60:11); [61:6](https://ref.ly/Isa61:6).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 23:18

## Isaiah 24:1 (id: 16038)

destroy the earth and make it a vast wasteland: There is a play here on the Hebrew words baqaq (*destroy*) and balaq (*make a wasteland*). The devastation will begin with Judah and be extended to the whole world (see [1 Pet 4:17](https://ref.ly/1Pet4:17)). • All the earth will be destroyed, the people as well as national political structures.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:1

## Isaiah 24:1–23 (id: 16030)

Here the judgments described in chs [13–23](https://ref.ly/Isa13:1-Isa23:18) are extended to the creation at large.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:1–ISA 24:23

## Isaiah 24:1–27.13 (id: 16031)

This section is often referred to as the “Little Apocalypse” because of its similarities to the book of Revelation. In these chapters Isaiah takes readers out of the present into a vision of the future world. The universal imagery of the Little Apocalypse makes it difficult to assign the events described to any precise historical situation. That means that these chapters cannot be used to outline a sequence of events or create a historical blueprint for the future. Instead, the imagery is intended to create an impressionistic drama of an unfolding world that is both like and unlike the present. The combination of aspects of the old era with aspects of the new era (for instance, people of Zion will all be righteous \[new], but still long for their redemption \[old]) is consistent with the New Testament concept of the future age breaking into and overlapping with the present evil age ([2 Cor 4:4](https://ref.ly/2Cor4:4); [Gal 1:4](https://ref.ly/Gal1:4)). Peter, for example, wrote of believers as living in the last days ([1 Pet 1:12](https://ref.ly/1Pet1:12); [2 Pet 3:3](https://ref.ly/2Pet3:3)) even though the last day remains yet future ([2 Pet 3:10](https://ref.ly/2Pet3:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:1–ISA 27:13

## Isaiah 24:4 (id: 16042)

earth . . . dries up . . . land wastes away and withers: The prophets often used the imagery of a drought to get the attention of people who live off the land (see also [Jer 23:10](https://ref.ly/Jer23:10); [Joel 1:12](https://ref.ly/Joel1:12); [Amos 1:2](https://ref.ly/Amos1:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:4

## Isaiah 24:4–13 (id: 16041)

All humanity is guilty ([Rom 3:23](https://ref.ly/Rom3:23)) and under condemnation (see [Isa 59:1–15](https://ref.ly/Isa59:1-Isa59:15)). Judgment is likened to a failed grape harvest that brings drinking and feasting to a halt.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:4–ISA 24:13

## Isaiah 24:5 (id: 16043)

Isaiah was probably referring to the everlasting covenant that God made with all humanity through Noah ([Gen 9:8–17](https://ref.ly/Gen9:8-Gen9:17)). The prophet saw a parallel with the wickedness in his day and the wickedness of the pre\-flood world.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:5

## Isaiah 24:6 (id: 16044)

The effect of sin is so great that no earthly thing or action can adequately atone for it. Hope ultimately lies in God himself. God has reconciled the world to himself in Christ ([Col 1:19–20](https://ref.ly/Col1:19-Col1:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:6

## Isaiah 24:7 (id: 16045)

Merrymakers will sigh and mourn because they are dependent on wine for pleasure.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:7

## Isaiah 24:8 (id: 16046)

Tambourines and melodious chords of the harp represent good times (see [5:12](https://ref.ly/Isa5:12); [Rev 18:22](https://ref.ly/Rev18:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:8

## Isaiah 24:10 (id: 16032)

city writhes in chaos: With normal levels of urban disarray being raised to chaotic and dangerous disorder, people will be forced to seek refuge in locked homes.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:10

## Isaiah 24:11 (id: 16033)

Gladness has been banished from the land*,* not by edict, but because there is no reason to celebrate.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:11

## Isaiah 24:13 (id: 16034)

stray olives left on the tree: The remnant will include godly people both from Israel (see [17:6](https://ref.ly/Isa17:6)) and from other nations. • The theme of the remnant is common in Isaiah (see “The Remnant” Theme Note; see also [10:22](https://ref.ly/Isa10:22); [14:30](https://ref.ly/Isa14:30); [16:14](https://ref.ly/Isa16:14); [24:6](https://ref.ly/Isa24:6); [37:32](https://ref.ly/Isa37:32)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:13
* **Associated Articles:** The Remnant (ID: `500293@TyndaleStudyNotesThemes`)

## Isaiah 24:14–16 (id: 16035)

In spite of wailing and ruin, the sound of praise is heard from all directions. Even while still experiencing the pain of the devastation of the old world, the godly will respond with joyous expectation for the new world.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:14–ISA 24:16

## Isaiah 24:16 (id: 16036)

But my heart is heavy with grief: The prophets were well acquainted with the emotional pain brought on by the sin of their people and its consequences (see [15:5](https://ref.ly/Isa15:5); [21:4](https://ref.ly/Isa21:4); [Jer 15:10](https://ref.ly/Jer15:10); [Mic 1:8](https://ref.ly/Mic1:8)). • Although aspects of the new world are present, the deceit of the old world still prevails for a time.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:16

## Isaiah 24:18 (id: 16037)

No one can escape the day of the Lord (see study note on 2:5–22). • Destruction falls like rain from the heavens (literally *the floodgates of heaven are opened*): The imagery is like the flood in Noah’s time (see study note on Gen 7:11–12). • the foundations of the earth shake: An earthquake is characteristic of a *theophany,* a physical manifestation of God’s presence (see also [Isa 6:4](https://ref.ly/Isa6:4); [13:13](https://ref.ly/Isa13:13); [19:1](https://ref.ly/Isa19:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:18
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 7:11-12 (ID: `12957@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Isaiah 2:5-22 (ID: `15969@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 24:21 (id: 16039)

The phrase translated gods (Hebrew tseba’ hammarom*,* “armed host of the heavens”) might refer to pagan gods represented by the stars. It might also refer to the angels or even demons ([Rev 12:4](https://ref.ly/Rev12:4), [9](https://ref.ly/Rev12:9)). Judgment will extend to all parts of creation ([Isa 13:13](https://ref.ly/Isa13:13); [34:5](https://ref.ly/Isa34:5); see [Eph 6:11–12](https://ref.ly/Eph6:11-Eph6:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:21

## Isaiah 24:22 (id: 16040)

put in prison . . . punished: See also [2 Pet 2:4](https://ref.ly/2Pet2:4); [Rev 9:2](https://ref.ly/Rev9:2), [11](https://ref.ly/Rev9:11); [17:8](https://ref.ly/Rev17:8).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 24:22

## Isaiah 25:1–5 (id: 16048)

Isaiah here praises God for protecting the needy (see [12:1–6](https://ref.ly/Isa12:1-Isa12:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:1–ISA 25:5

## Isaiah 25:1–12 (id: 16047)

The second part of the Little Apocalypse (see study note on 24:1–27:13) describes praise for God’s judgment ([25:1–5](https://ref.ly/Isa25:1-Isa25:5), [11–12](https://ref.ly/Isa25:11-Isa25:12)) and salvation ([25:6–9](https://ref.ly/Isa25:6-Isa25:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:1–ISA 25:12
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 24:1-27.13 (ID: `16031@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 25:3 (id: 16051)

Assyria and Egypt were strong . . . ruthless nations*,* but in the future they will exalt God rather than their own power.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:3

## Isaiah 25:6 (id: 16053)

In Jerusalem: Literally *On this mountain*—that is, the mountain of the Lord (see [2:2](https://ref.ly/Isa2:2); [24:23](https://ref.ly/Isa24:23)): This does not refer to a geographical city but to Zion, the eternal city of God (see [1:8](https://ref.ly/Isa1:8), [27](https://ref.ly/Isa1:27)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:6

## Isaiah 25:6–8 (id: 16052)

The banquet is a symbol of God’s providing richly for all those who will receive his gracious invitation ([Rev 3:20–21](https://ref.ly/Rev3:20-Rev3:21); [19:9](https://ref.ly/Rev19:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:6–ISA 25:8

## Isaiah 25:8 (id: 16054)

He will swallow up death forever: Isaiah’s hope was God’s power over sin and sin’s devastating results. In the New Testament this promise is realized in Jesus Christ ([1 Cor 15:54](https://ref.ly/1Cor15:54)). • The Lord’s presence will be a source of eternal comfort; he will wipe away all tears (see [Rev 21:4](https://ref.ly/Rev21:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:8

## Isaiah 25:9–12 (id: 16055)

The new community of God’s people will respond in joy and faith. When the enemy is defeated, God’s people will be free forever.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:9–ISA 25:12

## Isaiah 25:10 (id: 16049)

* *on this mountain*: This refers to Mount Zion in Jerusalem.
* Moab (see [Isaiah 15:1–16:14](https://ref.ly/Isa15:1-Isa16:14)) represents the nations facing judgment, those who rely only on themselves (see [Isaiah 34:5–17](https://ref.ly/Isa34:5-Isa34:17)).
* *as straw is trodden*: The people would be helpless and dying in very shameful circumstances.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:10

## Isaiah 25:11 (id: 16050)

Isaiah used the image of a swimmer forcibly kept under water to illustrate that Moab’s people are hopelessly doomed and their human pride will be conquered.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 25:11

## Isaiah 26:1 (id: 16065)

Our city refers to Zion, the eternal city of God, where the Lord is present to protect and bless his people (see [1:8](https://ref.ly/Isa1:8), [27](https://ref.ly/Isa1:27); see also [Ps 46](https://ref.ly/Ps46:1-Ps46:11)). Zion stands in direct contrast to the “mighty cities” of the nations ([Isa 25:2](https://ref.ly/Isa25:2); [26:5](https://ref.ly/Isa26:5)), which are helpless and are brought down to “ruins.” • The walls of God’s salvation protectively enclose his people; no one can hurt them again.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:1

## Isaiah 26:1–6 (id: 16057)

The new community of God’s people is likened to the citizens of a city, securely held together by the Lord. They are righteous, faithful, peaceful, and trusting.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:1–ISA 26:6

## Isaiah 26:1–21 (id: 16056)

This section of the Little Apocalypse (see study note on 24:1–27:13) is a song of praise and a prayer for redemption.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:1–ISA 26:21
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 24:1-27.13 (ID: `16031@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 26:2 (id: 16068)

Open access is given to all who*,* like God and his Messiah, are righteous and faithful. The citizens of true Zion are committed to the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:2

## Isaiah 26:3 (id: 16069)

A perfect peace, marked by harmony, quietness, and confidence, is a benefit of the new creation.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:3

## Isaiah 26:5 (id: 16070)

The proud and arrogant are often characterized as exalting themselves (see [2:11–12](https://ref.ly/Isa2:11-Isa2:12)), whereas God humbles them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:5

## Isaiah 26:6 (id: 16071)

Justice finally occurs when the needy walk all over the ruins of the city where the proud have perished.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:6

## Isaiah 26:7 (id: 16073)

God will do what is right for those who walk in his way, even if they have to suffer a while longer (see [24:16](https://ref.ly/Isa24:16)). One day, they will experience harmony and integrity (see [Prov 2:8–9](https://ref.ly/Prov2:8-Prov2:9); [15:19](https://ref.ly/Prov15:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:7

## Isaiah 26:7–21 (id: 16072)

The godly pray for the end of oppression and the full establishment of God’s kingdom. Isaiah encouraged the godly to persevere as they wait for their final vindication and the resurrection of the body.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:7–ISA 26:21

## Isaiah 26:8 (id: 16074)

we show our trust (see [26:3–4](https://ref.ly/Isa26:3-Isa26:4); literally *we wait for you*): Waiting involves trust in the Lord and longing for his redemption. • People express their commitment to God by obeying his laws; mere lip service is not sufficient.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:8

## Isaiah 26:9 (id: 16075)

I search . . . I earnestly seek: Cp. [Ps 63:1](https://ref.ly/Ps63:1).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:9

## Isaiah 26:10 (id: 16058)

God has shown kindness to the wicked. Although they deserve judgment (see also [Rom 2:4](https://ref.ly/Rom2:4); [2 Pet 3:9](https://ref.ly/2Pet3:9)), he has been patient and forbearing and has blessed them. However, time will run out one day.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:10

## Isaiah 26:11 (id: 16059)

An upraised fist expresses anger and the threat of judgment (see also [19:16](https://ref.ly/Isa19:16)). • Fire was often an image for God’s judgment.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:11

## Isaiah 26:15 (id: 16060)

This verse is a confident expression of thanks for God’s blessings. In Isaiah’s time, during King Hezekiah’s prosperous reign, the borders of the nation of Israel were extended*,* and people gave glory to God for his great deeds.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:15

## Isaiah 26:16 (id: 16061)

While waiting for God’s salvation ([26:1](https://ref.ly/Isa26:1)), the godly were in distress and longed for their vindication. They prayed beneath the burden of God’s discipline. In Hezekiah’s time, Judah was in distress when Sennacherib attacked and destroyed the whole nation except for Jerusalem.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:16

## Isaiah 26:17 (id: 16062)

The imagery of a pregnant woman in childbirth describes the nation’s pain when Assyrian king Sennacherib attacked it in 701 BC (see [37:3](https://ref.ly/Isa37:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:17

## Isaiah 26:18 (id: 16063)

Only God can give salvation and bring life (see [59:9–15](https://ref.ly/Isa59:9-Isa59:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:18

## Isaiah 26:19 (id: 16064)

those who die . . . will live: There is hope for the remnant. Some hold the minimal interpretation that this means the people would be restored to the land after the Exile (see [Ezek 37:11–12](https://ref.ly/Ezek37:11-Ezek37:12)) or that the Lord would preserve Jerusalem (see [Isa 29:4](https://ref.ly/Isa29:4), [6–8](https://ref.ly/Isa29:6-Isa29:8)). More likely it refers to the resurrection of the body from the grave (see [25:7–8](https://ref.ly/Isa25:7-Isa25:8)). The Old Testament has little to say about the resurrection, but this text anticipates fuller development in the New Testament (see [1 Cor 15:12–58](https://ref.ly/1Cor15:12-1Cor15:58)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:19

## Isaiah 26:20 (id: 16066)

God will punish the wicked, so the godly should go home and stay out of the way.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:20

## Isaiah 26:21 (id: 16067)

The wicked retain power now, but their oppressive rule will end in the day of the Lord’s wrath, when he will punish them for their sins.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 26:21

## Isaiah 27:1 (id: 16080)

Israel adapted the imagery of Leviathan to refer to evil powers that oppose God. Leviathan’s death symbolizes the end of evil, Satan, the demonic, and the dominion of forces hostile to God (see [51:9](https://ref.ly/Isa51:9); [Pss 74:14](https://ref.ly/Ps74:14); [104:7–9](https://ref.ly/Ps104:7-Ps104:9), [26](https://ref.ly/Ps104:26); see also the serpent in [Gen 3](https://ref.ly/Gen3:1-Gen3:24); [Rev 12](https://ref.ly/Rev12:1-Rev12:18); [13](https://ref.ly/Rev13:1-Rev13:18); [16:13](https://ref.ly/Rev16:13); [20:2](https://ref.ly/Rev20:2), [10](https://ref.ly/Rev20:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:1

## Isaiah 27:2–6 (id: 16081)

The Song of the Fruitful Vineyard, in which the vineyard represents God’s people (cp. [5:1–7](https://ref.ly/Isa5:1-Isa5:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:2–ISA 27:6

## Isaiah 27:3 (id: 16082)

God identifies himself as the Lord of the covenant to assure his people that what he says and does is reliable (see [Exod 6:2–3](https://ref.ly/Exod6:2-Exod6:3)). The phrase is used many times throughout Isaiah. • watch . . . watering . . . watch: The Lord will provide even greater care and protection for his ultimate vineyard than he did for Israel, his first vineyard (see [Isa 5:1–2](https://ref.ly/Isa5:1-Isa5:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:3

## Isaiah 27:5 (id: 16083)

God expects all his people to turn to him and to trust him for help. • Let them make peace with me is God’s offer of reconciliation for his people.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:5

## Isaiah 27:6 (id: 16084)

Jacob’s descendants will take root and the remnant will increase ([37:31](https://ref.ly/Isa37:31); cp. [5:24](https://ref.ly/Isa5:24); [14:30](https://ref.ly/Isa14:30); for application to the Messiah, see [11:1](https://ref.ly/Isa11:1), [10](https://ref.ly/Isa11:10)). • fill the whole earth: The fulfillment of this prophecy extends beyond the restoration of Israel from exile to the second coming of the Lord Jesus. It includes all the righteous children of God—Jews and Gentiles—who suffer but remain obedient as they wait for the fullness of redemption (see [26:18](https://ref.ly/Isa26:18); [John 15:1–8](https://ref.ly/John15:1-John15:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:6

## Isaiah 27:7 (id: 16086)

Despite being God’s covenant people, Israel was struck and punished like any other wicked nation. However, Israel was not completely destroyed; a remnant remained.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:7

## Isaiah 27:7–11 (id: 16085)

Isaiah reminded readers of the reasons for the Exile and judgment: Israel’s sinfulness, God’s righteous judgment, and the absence of divine compassion.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:7–ISA 27:11

## Isaiah 27:8 (id: 16087)

The Assyrians exiled large numbers of citizens from the northern kingdom of Israel when Samaria fell in 722 BC. The Babylonians exiled many from Judah from 605 to 586 BC. • Storms from the east were known for their destructive effects (see [Job 27:21](https://ref.ly/Job27:21); [38:24](https://ref.ly/Job38:24); [Jer 4:11](https://ref.ly/Jer4:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:8

## Isaiah 27:9 (id: 16088)

* God's justice requires him to punish sin to correct behavior and purify. The exile in Babylon happened to cleanse Israel's wickedness and remove all of Israel's sins (see [Romans 11:27](https://ref.ly/Rom11:27)).
* This aimed to end all pagan worship in Israel, including the incense altars and the poles used for worshiping the goddess Asherah.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:9

## Isaiah 27:10 (id: 16076)

Places previously associated with human power and accomplishment would be ruined to the extent that animals would graze in them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:10

## Isaiah 27:11 (id: 16077)

The people were outside of God’s covenantal care; they foolishly turned away from him.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:11

## Isaiah 27:12 (id: 16078)

Yet: God’s withholding of mercy from Israel ([27:11](https://ref.ly/Isa27:11)) will be temporary. • from the Euphrates River . . . to the Brook of Egypt: God’s remnant will return from Assyria and Egypt to the territory that God promised to Abraham ([Gen 15:18](https://ref.ly/Gen15:18); see [1 Kgs 4:21](https://ref.ly/1Kgs4:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:12

## Isaiah 27:13 (id: 16079)

The great trumpet was used to gather the people (see [Matt 24:31](https://ref.ly/Matt24:31)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 27:13

## Isaiah 28:1 (id: 16098)

The drunks of Israel epitomized pride, spiritual complacency, and scorn for God and his commands. • Unlike Jerusalem, Samaria was in a fertile valley.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:1

## Isaiah 28:1–29 (id: 16089)

The glory of Samaria, capital of the northern kingdom of Israel, is here contrasted with the glory of the Lord. This section represents the first of the six woes (What sorrow . . .).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:1–ISA 28:29

## Isaiah 28:1–33.24 (id: 16090)

This section is connected by a series of six threats of judgment or woes (cp. [5:8–23](https://ref.ly/Isa5:8-Isa5:23)). Apart from the initial address to Samaria in [28:1–13](https://ref.ly/Isa28:1-Isa28:13), the remainder of the section is addressed to Judah, especially because of their inclination to trust Egypt to rescue them from Assyria ([30:2](https://ref.ly/Isa30:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:1

## Isaiah 28:2 (id: 16106)

the Lord will send: The covenant God will act against his own people, whose behavior had effectively disqualified them as God’s people (see [Hos 1:9](https://ref.ly/Hos1:9)). • The mighty army is that of the Assyrians, who destroyed Samaria in 722 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:2

## Isaiah 28:5–6 (id: 16107)

In the future, after God’s judgment has purged his people, he will restore a righteous remnant, transforming the desires and character of his people. Samaria will no longer be the source of Israel’s pride; instead, they will glory in the Lord, their true crown.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:5–ISA 28:6

## Isaiah 28:7 (id: 16109)

The priests and prophets led the people astray. • Intoxicating beverages such as wine and alcohol were prohibited for priests while fulfilling their duties ([Lev 10:9](https://ref.ly/Lev10:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:7

## Isaiah 28:7–13 (id: 16108)

The focus returns to the lamentable present situation in Israel (and Judah). The leaders and people will be ensnared by their own schemes.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:7–ISA 28:13

## Isaiah 28:9–10 (id: 16110)

The people had become hardened to God’s revelation ([1 Cor 14:21](https://ref.ly/1Cor14:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:9–ISA 28:10

## Isaiah 28:11 (id: 16091)

God will have to speak through circumstances such as hardship, exile, and death in order to get through to his hardened people. • a strange language: In the New Testament, Paul applied this text to the spiritual gift of tongues as a sign of judgment to unbelievers (see [1 Cor 14:21–22](https://ref.ly/1Cor14:21-1Cor14:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:11

## Isaiah 28:12 (id: 16092)

Had his people listened, God’s revelation would have led them to rest (see [48:18–19](https://ref.ly/Isa48:18-Isa48:19)). But they would not listen because of their hard hearts ([65:12](https://ref.ly/Isa65:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:12

## Isaiah 28:13 (id: 16093)

one line at a time, one line at a time, a little here, and a little there: The Hebrew here is difficult to understand. The words might be intentional nonsense to illustrate the point that the people were so spiritually blind that God’s clearest revelation was nonsense to them (see [6:9–10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:9-Isa6:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:13

## Isaiah 28:14–22 (id: 16094)

After having pronounced judgment on Samaria ([28:1–13](https://ref.ly/Isa28:1-Isa28:13)), Isaiah now pronounces judgment on Jerusalem. The leaders of Judah are accused of being as blind as those in the northern kingdom of Israel.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:14–ISA 28:22

## Isaiah 28:15 (id: 16095)

cheat death (literally *have made a covenant with death*) . . . dodge the grave: There are two interpretive possibilities: (1\) The people had sold themselves to the Canaanite god of death, Mot, in return for his supposed protection. (2\) The prophet was being sarcastic; the people thought their various political and economic moves guaranteed their personal security, but they had actually destroyed themselves. • lies and deception: The leaders did not think of themselves as deceptive, but they were.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:15

## Isaiah 28:16 (id: 16096)

The Lord himself is the reliable foundation stone in Jerusalem, a precious cornerstone of the true city of God. There would be real hope in Jerusalem if the leaders and the people would only turn to him. If they would trust in God, they would be secure in the most terrible storm or the most devastating earthquake (see [8:14](https://ref.ly/Isa8:14); [Ps 118:22](https://ref.ly/Ps118:22); [Matt 7:24–27](https://ref.ly/Matt7:24-Matt7:27); [21:42](https://ref.ly/Matt21:42); see the application to Jesus Christ, [Rom 9:33](https://ref.ly/Rom9:33); [10:11](https://ref.ly/Rom10:11); [1 Cor 3:11](https://ref.ly/1Cor3:11); [Eph 2:20](https://ref.ly/Eph2:20); [1 Pet 2:4–7](https://ref.ly/1Pet2:4-1Pet2:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:16

## Isaiah 28:17 (id: 16097)

The Lord judges people on their works, which reveal the presence or absence of living faith (for justice and righteousness*,* see [1:21–23](https://ref.ly/Isa1:21-Isa1:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:17

## Isaiah 28:20 (id: 16099)

The bed. . . . The blankets: The leaders were ill\-prepared for God’s coming in judgment.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:20

## Isaiah 28:21 (id: 16100)

as he did . . . at Mount Perazim: See [2 Sam 5:20](https://ref.ly/2Sam5:20). • God had rescued Israel through a hailstorm at Gibeon ([Josh 10:10–12](https://ref.ly/Josh10:10-Josh10:12)), but now he would turn against them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:21

## Isaiah 28:23 (id: 16101)

Listen to me: The analogy that follows was written as a wisdom poem, with Isaiah calling on his audience to pay careful attention and to discern what is right.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:23

## Isaiah 28:24–29 (id: 16102)

A farmer knows that there are right and wrong ways to do things, but the leaders of Judah were not that intelligent.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:24–ISA 28:29

## Isaiah 28:27–28 (id: 16103)

The Lord has a variety of means (a heavy sledge . . . a light stick) for accomplishing his purposes. There are degrees of judgment.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:27–ISA 28:28

## Isaiah 28:28 (id: 16104)

he doesn’t keep on pounding it: This process would end; after judgment would come redemption.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:28

## Isaiah 28:29 (id: 16105)

wonderful teacher (or *counselor;* see [9:6](https://ref.ly/Isa9:6)): God’s people should learn wisdom about God’s ways, as the farmer has done.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 28:29

## Isaiah 29:1–2 (id: 16112)

Ariel was another name for Mount Zion ([29:8](https://ref.ly/Isa29:8)); it probably means *altar of God*.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:1–ISA 29:2

## Isaiah 29:1–8 (id: 16113)

In the Assyrian siege, the Lord would be fighting against Jerusalem, but he would force the Assyrians to abandon Jerusalem in his own time.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:1–ISA 29:8

## Isaiah 29:1–14 (id: 16111)

This is the second of the six woes (What sorrow . . .).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:1–ISA 29:14

## Isaiah 29:3 (id: 16125)

The Lord himself would come against Jerusalem, surrounding and attacking it.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:3

## Isaiah 29:4 (id: 16126)

God’s goal was to destroy the sinful pride of the people of Jerusalem. Their voice would rise as if from the grave. Through the Assyrians, God would humble his people, but he would not abandon them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:4

## Isaiah 29:6 (id: 16127)

will act for you (literally *you will be visited*): God would bring rescue for his people. • thunder and earthquake . . . storm and consuming fire: These phenomena indicate a theophany (see study note on 5:25).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:6
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 5:25 (ID: `16462@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 29:7 (id: 16128)

The Assyrians’ sudden lifting of the siege came like a dream . . . like a vision in the night. God would save Judah and judge Assyria.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:7

## Isaiah 29:8 (id: 16130)
