Like a hungry or thirsty person, the Assyrians could taste the victory over Jerusalem. Yet they never became victorious.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:8

## Isaiah 29:8–12 (id: 16129)

Jerusalem’s blindness kept its people from understanding God’s plan.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:8–ISA 29:12

## Isaiah 29:10 (id: 16114)

a spirit of deep sleep: The people’s folly was reinforced by God’s judgment on them (cp. [Rom 1:24–32](https://ref.ly/Rom1:24-Rom1:32); [11:8](https://ref.ly/Rom11:8)); they had no perception of reality. • Prophets were also sometimes called visionaries. Neither the people nor their prophets would understand what God was doing ([Isa 6:9–10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:9-Isa6:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:10

## Isaiah 29:13 (id: 16115)

These people used pious\-sounding language in their prayers and talk (see [Matt 15:8](https://ref.ly/Matt15:8); [Mark 7:6–7](https://ref.ly/Mark7:6-Mark7:7)), but they did not truly honor God. • In their hearts, they were not committed to the Lord at all. • In their worship, they followed man\-made rules and regulations rather than God’s word.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:13

## Isaiah 29:14 (id: 16116)

Human wisdom . . . intelligence . . . will disappear (see [1 Cor 1:19](https://ref.ly/1Cor1:19)). The prophet had already spoken of the failure of Egypt’s wise men ([Isa 19:11–12](https://ref.ly/Isa19:11-Isa19:12)); even Judah’s wise men would blunder.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:14

## Isaiah 29:15 (id: 16118)

Their plans might refer to the advice that royal counselors were giving Hezekiah, who at first attempted to free himself from Assyria by making alliances with Egypt (715 or 701 BC). • The people were conspiring to commit evil deeds in secret, but God saw everything.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:15

## Isaiah 29:15–24 (id: 16117)

This threat of coming judgment is the third of the six woes (see study note on 28:1–33:24). It begins with judgment but moves to a vision of creation being renewed and of the wicked coming to an end.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:15–ISA 29:24
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 28:1-33.24 (ID: `16090@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 29:16 (id: 16119)

Potter . . . clay: The Lord’s sovereignty is beyond challenge. Scripture does not discourage asking God hard questions, but there is no place for resistance to God’s will (see [10:15](https://ref.ly/Isa10:15); [45:9](https://ref.ly/Isa45:9); [64:8](https://ref.ly/Isa64:8); [Rom 9:20](https://ref.ly/Rom9:20)). • He didn’t make me: Such claims against God demonstrate a total unwillingness to recognize God’s intimate involvement with every aspect of a person’s life.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:16

## Isaiah 29:17 (id: 16120)

The forests of Lebanon are usually an image of luxuriant growth (see [2:13](https://ref.ly/Isa2:13); [14:8](https://ref.ly/Isa14:8)), but here they represent desolation.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:17

## Isaiah 29:18 (id: 16121)

The people were deaf and blind in heart and spirit (see [6:10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:10); [42:18](https://ref.ly/Isa42:18); [43:8](https://ref.ly/Isa43:8)). Yet humanity and all of creation would be renewed (see [35:1–5](https://ref.ly/Isa35:1-Isa35:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:18

## Isaiah 29:21 (id: 16122)

The false testimony that led to the oppression of the poor through trickery in the courts of Isaiah’s era would end. Because of God’s work in their hearts, the people would turn from their sinful behavior of the past.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:21

## Isaiah 29:22 (id: 16124)

Abraham was the father of all Israel (see [Gen 12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3); see also [Gal 3:29](https://ref.ly/Gal3:29)). • Rescued from human abuse and God’s judgment, the people would no longer be ashamed; their disgrace resulted from the apparent failure of what they had trusted (see [Ps 71:1](https://ref.ly/Ps71:1); [1 Pet 2:6](https://ref.ly/1Pet2:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:22

## Isaiah 29:22–24 (id: 16123)

The prophecy of woe, which began at [29:15](https://ref.ly/Isa29:15), now ends with a prophecy of salvation.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 29:22–ISA 29:24

## Isaiah 30:1 (id: 16158)

The wisdom of the people of Judah was not directed by God’s Spirit (cp. [11:2](https://ref.ly/Isa11:2)); instead, they had formed alliances that God did not approve of. Although the Assyrian attack on Jerusalem was overwhelming, making an alliance with Egypt demonstrated that the leaders of Judah depended on human resources for their protection rather than on God. Fortunately, Hezekiah later had a change of heart (see chs [36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa37:38)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:1

## Isaiah 30:1–5 (id: 16148)

This prophecy was against Judah’s dependence on Egypt. It is the fourth of the six woes (see study note on 28:1–33:24).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:1–ISA 30:5
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 28:1-33.24 (ID: `16090@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 30:1–33 (id: 16147)

After threatening Judah with judgment because of their stubbornness and their reliance on Egypt ([30:1–17](https://ref.ly/Isa30:1-Isa30:17)), God again committed himself to deal compassionately with his people and to break the power of their enemies.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:1–ISA 30:33

## Isaiah 30:4 (id: 16170)

Zoan: See [19:11](https://ref.ly/Isa19:11). The location of Hanes is uncertain.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:4

## Isaiah 30:6 (id: 16172)

Caravans moved from Judah through the Negev and the Sinai Peninsula to Egypt to avoid the main coast road that was under Assyrian control. It was a dangerous region, filled with lions and poisonous snakes. People would risk their lives to make a worthless alliance. • Being weighed down with riches might refer to money being sent to Egypt to buy that nation’s help.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:6

## Isaiah 30:6–7 (id: 16171)

This taunt was against those who were busy trying to please the Egyptians in order to get Egypt’s military support.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:6–ISA 30:7

## Isaiah 30:7 (id: 16173)

Harmless Dragon: In ancient mythology, Rahab was the enemy of the good gods and was depicted in fearsome terms, somewhat like a giant crocodile (see [27:1](https://ref.ly/Isa27:1); [51:9](https://ref.ly/Isa51:9); [Job 41:1–34](https://ref.ly/Job41:1-Job41:34)). By saying that Egypt was *harmless,* the prophet meant that it would be of no help to Judah.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:7

## Isaiah 30:8–11 (id: 16174)

God instructed the prophet to write down the vision for future generations. The present generation was rebellious; they rejected God’s word in favor of the false prophets’ fantasies (see[8:16–17](https://ref.ly/Isa8:16-Isa8:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:8–ISA 30:11

## Isaiah 30:11 (id: 16149)

Stop telling us: The people of Judah did not want to be confronted about sin or judgment.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:11

## Isaiah 30:12 (id: 16151)

The whole society accepted oppression and lies. People reinforced each other’s delusions, so it was considered acceptable to trust that Egypt would protect them from Assyria, even though that was a lie. It was also considered legal and legitimate to oppress the poor by taking their land.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:12

## Isaiah 30:12–17 (id: 16150)

Judgment would suddenly overtake the people of Judah because they were content with their fantasy world, and they refused God’s path to rest and quietness.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:12–ISA 30:17

## Isaiah 30:15 (id: 16152)

The people of Judah needed to repent of their sinful ways ([30:12–14](https://ref.ly/Isa30:12-Isa30:14)), returning to the Lord, in order to be rescued. • Trust in the Lord would bring quietness and confidence*,* unlike their frantic negotiations with Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:15

## Isaiah 30:16 (id: 16153)

Judah’s reliance on Egypt to supply swift horses (see [Ps 33:17](https://ref.ly/Ps33:17)) amounted to a rejection of God’s help and threatened to bring about its fall.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:16

## Isaiah 30:17 (id: 16154)

One . . . Five: This curse is the opposite of God’s blessing for obedience ([Lev 26:8](https://ref.ly/Lev26:8); [Deut 32:30](https://ref.ly/Deut32:30)). • The Assyrians had dominated the rest of the land of Judah, leaving Jerusalem isolated like a lonely flagpole on a hill.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:17

## Isaiah 30:18 (id: 16156)

A faithful God would restore righteous order to the world by punishing the wicked and rescuing his people from them (see [1:27](https://ref.ly/Isa1:27); [Ps 96:10–12](https://ref.ly/Ps96:10-Ps96:12)). • God blesses those who wait; faithful people do not rush ahead of him to solve their own problems but instead rely on his power and goodness.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:18

## Isaiah 30:18–33 (id: 16155)

This prophecy of salvation includes promises that directed Israel’s attention away from their present adversity to the glorious future awaiting the children of God.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:18–ISA 30:33

## Isaiah 30:19 (id: 16157)

This was probably a challenge and promise to Hezekiah during the Assyrian siege of Jerusalem (see chs [36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa37:38)). If he would just trust in God, his people would weep no more. • The Lord cares for the needs of his people; when they ask for something, he will surely respond.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:19

## Isaiah 30:20 (id: 16159)

adversity for food and suffering for drink: A city under enemy siege for a long time would eventually be overcome by starvation and disease. God did not intend that this disaster should destroy the people of Jerusalem, rather that it would be a tool to teach them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:20

## Isaiah 30:21 (id: 16160)

The people’s own ears will hear and follow God’s instructions (see [6:9–10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:9-Isa6:10); [29:18](https://ref.ly/Isa29:18); [35:5](https://ref.ly/Isa35:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:21

## Isaiah 30:22 (id: 16161)

One aspect of Hezekiah’s reforms was to remove silver idols and other idolatry of Ahaz’s era and to call the people back to the worship of the God of Israel ([2 Kgs 18:4–5](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:4-2Kgs18:5); [2 Chr 29](https://ref.ly/2Chr29:1-2Chr29:36)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:22

## Isaiah 30:23–24 (id: 16162)

The people of Judah would again enjoy covenant blessings rather than curses (see [Lev 26:4](https://ref.ly/Lev26:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:23–ISA 30:24

## Isaiah 30:26 (id: 16163)

The Lord who struck his people would also heal them and cure the wounds he gave them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:26

## Isaiah 30:27–33 (id: 16164)

In this prophecy of judgment against the nations, Isaiah foretold that Assyria’s power would be broken by God’s strong arm, not by the Egyptians.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:27–ISA 30:33

## Isaiah 30:28 (id: 16165)

like a flood up to the neck: Assyria’s destruction would be as devastating as their invasion of Judah (see [8:8](https://ref.ly/Isa8:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:28

## Isaiah 30:30 (id: 16166)

His mighty arm presents an image of strength that echoes God’s victory over the Egyptians in the Exodus ([63:12](https://ref.ly/Isa63:12); see [Exod 15:6](https://ref.ly/Exod15:6)). • devouring flames . . . huge hailstones: Such phenomena are associated with God’s appearance in a theophany (see study note on Isa 5:25).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:30
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 5:25 (ID: `16462@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 30:31 (id: 16167)

royal scepter: The Lord, the true King, would be victorious over the threatening Assyrian king.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:31

## Isaiah 30:32 (id: 16168)

tambourines and harps: Instruments formerly silenced in judgment ([24:8](https://ref.ly/Isa24:8)) would again be used by God’s people; this time, the people would celebrate the Lord’s goodness rather than becoming spiritually complacent (see [5:12](https://ref.ly/Isa5:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:32

## Isaiah 30:33 (id: 16169)

Topheth was a pagan altar in the valley of Ben\-Hinnom outside Jerusalem where child sacrifices were offered to the Ammonite god Molech by burning ([2 Kgs 23:9–10](https://ref.ly/2Kgs23:9-2Kgs23:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 30:33

## Isaiah 31:1 (id: 16176)

What sorrow: This is the fifth of the six woes (see study note on 28:1–33:24). • Looking to the Lord implies seeking his guidance with a worshipful attitude (see [Deut 4:29](https://ref.ly/Deut4:29); [Ps 105:3–4](https://ref.ly/Ps105:3-Ps105:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 31:1
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 28:1-33.24 (ID: `16090@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 31:1–3 (id: 16175)

Isaiah delivered another vision regarding Judah’s negotiations with Egypt, which probably took place in 705–701 BC. During that time, the conquering Assyrians were on the move, threatening Judah. At first, Hezekiah tried political and military means to avert being totally conquered (see [2 Kgs 18:13–16](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:13-2Kgs18:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 31:1–ISA 31:3

## Isaiah 31:4–9 (id: 16177)

This prophecy concerned God’s protection of Jerusalem and its rescue from the Assyrians.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 31:4–ISA 31:9

## Isaiah 31:9 (id: 16178)

princes will flee: This was fulfilled when Sennacherib fled to Nineveh after God destroyed 185,000 troops (see[37:36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa37:36-Isa37:37)). • God himself was the fire or flame that would devour Assyria.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 31:9

## Isaiah 32:1 (id: 16186)

a righteous king: The Messiah ([11:1](https://ref.ly/Isa11:1)); the emphasis now is on righteous and wise leadership ([Prov 8:20](https://ref.ly/Prov8:20)). • In Isaiah’s day, honest princes were few and far between (see [Isa 3:1–6](https://ref.ly/Isa3:1-Isa3:6); [28:7–19](https://ref.ly/Isa28:7-Isa28:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:1

## Isaiah 32:2 (id: 16187)

like a shelter . . . a refuge: The same words were also used to describe God’s protection ([25:4](https://ref.ly/Isa25:4)) and his provision of life\-giving water ([41:18](https://ref.ly/Isa41:18); [43:19–20](https://ref.ly/Isa43:19-Isa43:20)). The leaders would serve as God’s agents in blessing his people.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:2

## Isaiah 32:5 (id: 16188)

Only foolish people value fools and scoundrels as heroes (see also [19:11](https://ref.ly/Isa19:11), [13](https://ref.ly/Isa19:13); [32:6–8](https://ref.ly/Isa32:6-Isa32:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:5

## Isaiah 32:6 (id: 16189)

Because they do not care about God’s standards of justice and righteousness, fools have no regard for those who are hungry or thirsty (see [1:17](https://ref.ly/Isa1:17); [58:7](https://ref.ly/Isa58:7), [10](https://ref.ly/Isa58:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:6

## Isaiah 32:7 (id: 16190)

smooth tricks . . . crooked schemes: See [Prov 6:12](https://ref.ly/Prov6:12), [18](https://ref.ly/Prov6:18); [16:27](https://ref.ly/Prov16:27); [24:8–9](https://ref.ly/Prov24:8-Prov24:9).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:7

## Isaiah 32:9 (id: 16192)

The women of Jerusalem lie around in ease. Complacent in their high standard of life and low standard of morality, they put their trust in wealth and status to maintain their way of life.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:9

## Isaiah 32:9–15 (id: 16191)

This prophecy of judgment was against the complacent women of Jerusalem. God’s promises regarding the future did not justify continuing in sin.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:9–ISA 32:15

## Isaiah 32:10 (id: 16179)

In a short time: Assyria’s siege of Jerusalem (701 BC) was looming.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:10

## Isaiah 32:11 (id: 16180)

Burlap was worn to show . . . grief*,* especially when mourning (see [Ps 30:11](https://ref.ly/Ps30:11)). The Assyrian conquest of the farmlands of Judah and most of its cities (except Jerusalem) would cause rich people in the large cities to mourn.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:11

## Isaiah 32:14 (id: 16181)

Jerusalem was eventually destroyed, but by the Babylonians, not the Assyrians (for a prediction of this, see [39:1–8](https://ref.ly/Isa39:1-Isa39:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:14

## Isaiah 32:15 (id: 16182)

The Spirit would transform the nation into a godly community and bring in an era of justice and righteousness (see also [Joel 2:28–32](https://ref.ly/Joel2:28-Joel2:32)). The Spirit is connected to the Messiah ([Isa 11:1](https://ref.ly/Isa11:1)) and the servant ([42:1](https://ref.ly/Isa42:1); [61:1](https://ref.ly/Isa61:1)). • wilderness . . . bountiful crops: When the people are transformed, nature will be too.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:15

## Isaiah 32:17 (id: 16183)

Being right with God and humans will bring peace (Hebrew shalom; see [26:3](https://ref.ly/Isa26:3); [48:18](https://ref.ly/Isa48:18); [52:7](https://ref.ly/Isa52:7); cp. [Jas 3:18](https://ref.ly/Jas3:18)). Peace is more than the absence of conflict. It includes personal wholeness and does not depend on outside circumstances (see also [Rom 5:1](https://ref.ly/Rom5:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:17

## Isaiah 32:18 (id: 16184)

Living in safety and feeling at rest were blessings of Israel’s covenant with the Lord (see [Lev 26:5–6](https://ref.ly/Lev26:5-Lev26:6)), but the people’s sin had previously brought curses rather than blessings (cp. [Isa 32:9–11](https://ref.ly/Isa32:9-Isa32:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:18

## Isaiah 32:19 (id: 16185)

the forest . . . the city: The godly would remain secure even with evidence of God’s judgment all around them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 32:19

## Isaiah 33:1 (id: 16200)

This was the sixth threat of woe (see study note on 28:1–33:24). • Although the Hebrew text does not specifically name Assyria as the object of the prophecy in this chapter, Isaiah undoubtedly had Assyria in mind; they were the *destroyer* most immediately at hand when the prophecy was written. The prophecy applies, however, to any who seek to destroy God’s people; this included, but was not limited to, the Babylonians (see chs [13–14](https://ref.ly/Isa13:1-Isa14:32)). • When you are done betraying: Wicked nations often break political agreements with other nations when these contracts are no longer to their own advantage.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:1
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 28:1-33.24 (ID: `16090@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 33:2 (id: 16202)

The godly community prayed for God to be merciful in response to the promise of [30:18](https://ref.ly/Isa30:18).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:2

## Isaiah 33:9 (id: 16203)

Sharon, Bashan, and Carmel were all fertile areas. Their desolation represents the destruction Assyria had inflicted on Israel.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:9

## Isaiah 33:11 (id: 16193)

In most passages, the Assyrians were turning other nations into worthless dry grass and stubble (see [17:13](https://ref.ly/Isa17:13); [29:5](https://ref.ly/Isa29:5); [40:24](https://ref.ly/Isa40:24); [41:2](https://ref.ly/Isa41:2)). Here, ironically, they get a taste of their own medicine.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:11

## Isaiah 33:14 (id: 16194)

The godless were people who lived without regard for God and his law. • The image of a devouring fire came from God’s judgment of offenders during the wilderness journey ([Exod 24:17](https://ref.ly/Exod24:17); [Deut 4:24](https://ref.ly/Deut4:24)). God still expects people to worship him with reverence and awe ([Heb 12:29](https://ref.ly/Heb12:29)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:14

## Isaiah 33:15 (id: 16195)

A godly life provides evidence that an individual truly knows God (see [Col 3:5–17](https://ref.ly/Col3:5-Col3:17); [Jas 2:14–18](https://ref.ly/Jas2:14-Jas2:18); [1 Pet 1:14–16](https://ref.ly/1Pet1:14-1Pet1:16); [1 Jn 1:5–6](https://ref.ly/1John1:5-1John1:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:15

## Isaiah 33:17 (id: 16197)

The king is the human representative ([32:1](https://ref.ly/Isa32:1)) of the Great King, God himself ([33:22](https://ref.ly/Isa33:22)). • The land belonging to God’s people would appear to be without borders because the foreign enemies had been judged ([54:3](https://ref.ly/Isa54:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:17

## Isaiah 33:17–24 (id: 16196)

This vision is about God’s reign in Zion.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:17–ISA 33:24

## Isaiah 33:18 (id: 16198)

Assyrian officers brought great terror to Judah when the Assyrians defeated various cities around Jerusalem ([2 Kgs 18:13](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:18

## Isaiah 33:19 (id: 16199)

The strange, unknown language was the tongue of Assyria and later of Babylon (see [28:11](https://ref.ly/Isa28:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:19

## Isaiah 33:24 (id: 16201)

The people of Israel were the people of Zion (cp. [33:20](https://ref.ly/Isa33:20)). • sick . . . the Lord will forgive their sins: See [43:25](https://ref.ly/Isa43:25).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 33:24

## Isaiah 34:1–17 (id: 16204)

This prophecy of judgment was against the nations at the terrifying day of the Lord (see study note on 2:5–22; see also[63:1–6](https://ref.ly/Isa63:1-Isa63:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:1–ISA 34:17
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 2:5-22 (ID: `15969@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 34:2 (id: 16209)

completely destroy: See [Lev 27:28–29](https://ref.ly/Lev27:28-Lev27:29).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:2

## Isaiah 34:3 (id: 16210)

It was a disgrace for a corpse to remain unburied.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:3

## Isaiah 34:4 (id: 16211)

The heavens . . . stars: See also [13:10](https://ref.ly/Isa13:10); [Joel 2:30–31](https://ref.ly/Joel2:30-Joel2:31); [Matt 24:29](https://ref.ly/Matt24:29); [Mark 13:24–25](https://ref.ly/Mark13:24-Mark13:25); [2 Pet 3:10–13](https://ref.ly/2Pet3:10-2Pet3:13); [Rev 6:13–14](https://ref.ly/Rev6:13-Rev6:14).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:4

## Isaiah 34:5 (id: 16212)

Edom here represents what all the nations would experience, just as Moab was singled out in ch [25](https://ref.ly/Isa25:1-Isa25:12).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:5

## Isaiah 34:6 (id: 16213)

Bozrah was a city in Edom, southeast of the Dead Sea.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:6

## Isaiah 34:8 (id: 16214)

The Lord’s revenge refers to God’s wrathful but righteous vengeance against his enemies and the oppressors of his people on the day of the Lord. • Edom will be paid back: There will be retribution for Edom’s crimes against Judah (see also [Ps 137:7](https://ref.ly/Ps137:7); [Lam 4:21](https://ref.ly/Lam4:21); Obad; [Mal 1:3–5](https://ref.ly/Mal1:3-Mal1:5)). Edom, with its history of treachery, would come to an end.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:8

## Isaiah 34:9 (id: 16215)

Edom’s judgment was similar to the judgment of Sodom and Gomorrah ([Gen 19:24](https://ref.ly/Gen19:24); see also [Jer 49:17–18](https://ref.ly/Jer49:17-Jer49:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:9

## Isaiah 34:10 (id: 16205)

See also [Rev 14:11](https://ref.ly/Rev14:11); [19:3](https://ref.ly/Rev19:3).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:10

## Isaiah 34:11 (id: 16207)

The identification of some of these birds is uncertain, but they inhabited wild and deserted places and many were considered unclean ([Deut 14:11–18](https://ref.ly/Deut14:11-Deut14:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:11

## Isaiah 34:11–14 (id: 16206)

Wild animals, night birds, and hostile plants with thorns and nettles created an eerie environment.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:11–ISA 34:14

## Isaiah 34:16 (id: 16208)

Search the book of the Lord: Isaiah’s prophecy was God’s witness to the truthfulness of his word. • The power of the Spirit enacts the Lord’s decrees, assuring that all prophecy comes together to be fulfilled as God promised.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 34:16

## Isaiah 35:1 (id: 16218)

God’s land would become like a wasteland, spiritually desolate and physically depleted. However, God would turn the desert into a garden (see also [32:15](https://ref.ly/Isa32:15)), taking his people from exile to glory.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:1

## Isaiah 35:1–10 (id: 16216)

God will come to vindicate and transform his people. Instead of being deaf, blind, and lame (see [6:10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:10)), the people will be pure, holy, and redeemed.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:1–ISA 35:10

## Isaiah 35:2 (id: 16219)

Lebanon . . . Mount Carmel . . . Sharon: There will be a reversal of earlier judgments (see [2:13](https://ref.ly/Isa2:13); [10:34](https://ref.ly/Isa10:34); [29:17](https://ref.ly/Isa29:17); [33:9](https://ref.ly/Isa33:9)). • glory . . . splendor of our God: See also [6:3](https://ref.ly/Isa6:3); [40:5](https://ref.ly/Isa40:5).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:2

## Isaiah 35:3 (id: 16220)

Tired hands and weak knees express discouragement and anxiety (see [Heb 12:12](https://ref.ly/Heb12:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:3

## Isaiah 35:4 (id: 16221)

do not fear: The Lord here assures his people of his presence and purpose (see also [7:4](https://ref.ly/Isa7:4); [10:24](https://ref.ly/Isa10:24); [40:9](https://ref.ly/Isa40:9); [43:1](https://ref.ly/Isa43:1); [44:2](https://ref.ly/Isa44:2); [54:4](https://ref.ly/Isa54:4)). • The essence of the good news is that God is coming to save his people (see also [40:9](https://ref.ly/Isa40:9); [52:7](https://ref.ly/Isa52:7); [62:11](https://ref.ly/Isa62:11)). God’s coming brings spiritual transformation as well as rescue from enemies.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:4

## Isaiah 35:5 (id: 16222)

eyes . . . ears: There will be a transformation from spiritual blindness and deafness to understanding and knowing God ([6:10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:10); [29:18](https://ref.ly/Isa29:18); [32:3](https://ref.ly/Isa32:3); [42:7](https://ref.ly/Isa42:7); see also [Luke 7:22](https://ref.ly/Luke7:22); [Acts 26:18](https://ref.ly/Acts26:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:5

## Isaiah 35:6 (id: 16223)

The lame . . . those who cannot speak: All disabilities will be removed (see [Matt 11:5](https://ref.ly/Matt11:5); [12:22](https://ref.ly/Matt12:22); [Mark 7:37](https://ref.ly/Mark7:37); [Acts 3:7–8](https://ref.ly/Acts3:7-Acts3:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:6

## Isaiah 35:8 (id: 16224)

The imagery of the great road recalls Israel’s crossing through the Red Sea ([51:10](https://ref.ly/Isa51:10)). • Only holy and undefiled people will share the Highway of Holiness. God’s goal in salvation is that humans will share in his character and act as he does (see [Eph 5:1–2](https://ref.ly/Eph5:1-Eph5:2); [1 Pet 2:21](https://ref.ly/1Pet2:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:8

## Isaiah 35:10 (id: 16217)

Jerusalem: The prophecy extends beyond the return of Israel from exile to the final establishment of God’s kingdom on earth. It will be characterized by joy and gladness and the absence of sorrow and mourning (see also [25:7](https://ref.ly/Isa25:7); [51:11](https://ref.ly/Isa51:11); [Rev 21:4](https://ref.ly/Rev21:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 35:10

## Isaiah 36:1 (id: 16233)

King Sennacherib of Assyria ruled from 705–681 BC. Hezekiah, like many other kings under Assyrian vassalage, had reasserted his independence when Sennacherib came to the throne in troubled circumstances. By 701 BC, the Assyrian king was ready to punish Hezekiah. Sennacherib recorded having conquered forty\-six fortified cities and many villages, and having taken 200,146 captives. Hezekiah responded by attempting to appease Sennacherib (see [2 Kgs 18:14–16](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:14-2Kgs18:16)), but it was too late.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:1

## Isaiah 36:1–39.8 (id: 16225)

Jerusalem’s rescue from King Sennacherib of Assyria is a focal point of chs [1–39](https://ref.ly/Isa1:1-Isa39:8). The Lord promised to rescue his people by remaining present with Jerusalem (see [7:14](https://ref.ly/Isa7:14)) and by maintaining a remnant (see study note on 10:20). Even though much of the country was desolate and the population decimated because of their foolish alliance with Assyria (chs [7–8](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa8:22)), the Lord preserved his people from total conquest through Hezekiah, a godly king. Hezekiah faced the same test that Ahaz did, a test of trust (note the recurrence of words for *trust* in the Assyrian officer’s challenge in ch [36](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa36:22)). But Hezekiah has learned the lessons taught in chs [13–35](https://ref.ly/Isa13:1-Isa35:10) and, at least initially, he passed the test. This narrative is duplicated in [2 Kgs 18:13–20:19](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:13-2Kgs20:19).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:1–ISA 37:38
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 10:20 (ID: `15824@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 36:2 (id: 16234)

Lachish was a city overlooking the low\-lying hills to the west of Jerusalem. It had to be taken before the final attack on Jerusalem could be launched. • the aqueduct that feeds water into the upper pool: This was a pool on the north side of Jerusalem, not the Gihon Spring in the Kidron valley. • Isaiah had met Ahaz on this same road leading to the field where cloth is washed some thirty\-three years earlier (see [Isa 7:3](https://ref.ly/Isa7:3)). At that time he had challenged Ahaz to trust God. However, Ahaz trusted Assyria instead, and this desperate situation was a result. Now the challenge to trust God came from mocking, foreign lips (see [28:11–13](https://ref.ly/Isa28:11-Isa28:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:2

## Isaiah 36:3 (id: 16235)

Eliakim eventually advanced in rank, while Shebna was demoted (see [22:15–24](https://ref.ly/Isa22:15-Isa22:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:3

## Isaiah 36:4 (id: 16237)

Great king is a title similar to *emperor.*

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:4

## Isaiah 36:4–22 (id: 16236)

The Assyrian chief of staff attempted to use intimidation to negotiate a settlement without bloodshed. In his first speech ([36:4–10](https://ref.ly/Isa36:4-Isa36:10)), he rightly argued against Egypt’s ability to rescue but wrongly charged Hezekiah with misplaced trust in the Lord. Strikingly, the Assyrian did not see the contest as being between the gods of Assyria and the Lord but rather between Sennacherib—the great king—and the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:4–ISA 36:22

## Isaiah 36:5 (id: 16238)

Who are you counting on: Judah had asked Egypt to help them (see [30:1–5](https://ref.ly/Isa30:1-Isa30:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:5

## Isaiah 36:6 (id: 16239)

Egypt . . . is completely unreliable: Isaiah argued the same case, pointing to the Lord as the only reliable source of help ([31:1–3](https://ref.ly/Isa31:1-Isa31:3), [7](https://ref.ly/Isa31:7); [39:7](https://ref.ly/Isa39:7)). • A kind of reed that breaks easily grows near the Nile (see [Ezek 29:6–7](https://ref.ly/Ezek29:6-Ezek29:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:6

## Isaiah 36:7 (id: 16240)

We are trusting in the Lord our God: Ironically, the Assyrian chief of staff knew about and played on Isaiah’s message ([26:4](https://ref.ly/Isa26:4); [36:15](https://ref.ly/Isa36:15); [37:10](https://ref.ly/Isa37:10)). • Hezekiah showed his zeal for the Lord by tearing down the pagan shrines and altars ([2 Kgs 18:4](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:4)), but the Assyrians mistakenly thought Hezekiah had angered God in the process.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:7

## Isaiah 36:8 (id: 16241)

At this time, cavalry mounted on horses was the newest military technology. The Assyrian chief of staff was mocking the Judeans because he knew that Judah had no trained men who knew how to ride.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:8

## Isaiah 36:10 (id: 16226)

The Lord himself told us: It was common for a king to claim the approval of a deity in destroying other kingdoms. This claim was consistent with Isaiah’s prophecy in [10:5–6](https://ref.ly/Isa10:5-Isa10:6).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:10

## Isaiah 36:11 (id: 16227)

Aramaic was the lingua franca used throughout the Assyrian Empire, understood by the elite but not by the common people of Judah.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:11

## Isaiah 36:13–20 (id: 16228)

In the Assyrian chief of staff’s second speech, he claimed that his nation’s gods were more powerful than the God of Judah (cp. [10:9–10](https://ref.ly/Isa10:9-Isa10:10)). God’s ability to rescue his people was at stake. In the ensuing drama, the Lord shocked the Assyrian with his power ([37:36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa37:36-Isa37:37)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:13–ISA 36:20

## Isaiah 36:14 (id: 16229)

Don’t let Hezekiah deceive you: This public accusation against Hezekiah was intended to undermine the people’s confidence in him.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:14

## Isaiah 36:15 (id: 16230)

Hezekiah demonstrated a strong faith in the Lord, unlike Ahaz (ch [7](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa7:25)). Hezekiah’s faith would bring rescue*,* whereas Ahaz’s lack of faith brought him trouble (see [7:9](https://ref.ly/Isa7:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:15

## Isaiah 36:16–17 (id: 16231)

The Assyrian was tempting the people of Jerusalem with provisions that the Lord had promised to provide his obedient people (see [1:19](https://ref.ly/Isa1:19); [37:30–35](https://ref.ly/Isa37:30-Isa37:35); [Deut 8:7–9](https://ref.ly/Deut8:7-Deut8:9)). It was a tempting offer, but a counterfeit. And it covered up the reality of exile that would have followed ([Isa 36:17](https://ref.ly/Isa36:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:16–ISA 36:17

## Isaiah 36:19 (id: 16232)

The populations of Hamath and Arpad had been relocated to Samaria, where the new local deities were the gods of Sepharvaim ([2 Kgs 17:24](https://ref.ly/2Kgs17:24)). The logic of the argument made sense to an Assyrian. If the Assyrian gods defeated all the gods of the other nations and the God of the Israelite nation of Samaria, would they not also defeat the God of Judah?

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 36:19

## Isaiah 37:1 (id: 16245)

Hezekiah responded to the threats by seeking the Lord’s favor. • The king tore his clothes and put on burlap as a sign of mourning and prayer.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:1

## Isaiah 37:4 (id: 16262)

perhaps . . . God has heard the Assyrian chief of staff . . . defy the living God: Hezekiah’s hope was not based on Judah’s or his own goodness, or the presence of the Temple in Jerusalem; instead, it was based on God responding to Assyria’s blasphemy.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:4

## Isaiah 37:5–7 (id: 16263)

God answered that he would deal with the Assyrians and rescue the remnant of his people from their distress (see [63:9](https://ref.ly/Isa63:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:5–ISA 37:7

## Isaiah 37:8 (id: 16264)

Libnah was a city on the Philistine plain near Lachish. Sennacherib was preparing to fight both Egypt and Jerusalem when he sent word to Hezekiah.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:8

## Isaiah 37:9 (id: 16265)

King Tirhakah of Ethiopia was then the ruler of Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:9

## Isaiah 37:12 (id: 16242)

The argument was that Judah’s god, like the gods of other nations already conquered by Assyria, would be unable to save Judah (see also [36:18–20](https://ref.ly/Isa36:18-Isa36:20)). • Gozan, Haran, Rezeph, . . . Eden, and Tel\-assar were cities in Mesopotamia.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:12

## Isaiah 37:14–20 (id: 16243)

When he received Sennacherib’s boastful threat, Hezekiah returned to the Temple and prayed for rescue. His prayer stands in contrast to Ahaz’s response to danger decades earlier (see ch [7](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa7:25)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:14–ISA 37:20

## Isaiah 37:16 (id: 16244)

God of Israel: Hezekiah was not taken in by the false claims of Sennacherib that the Lord was powerless. In his prayer, Hezekiah acknowledged that, because the Lord is the Creator, he is God of all the kingdoms of the earth. The Lord is sovereign over all kingdoms. • The mighty cherubim were located in the Temple atop the Ark of the Covenant, which was God’s throne in his Temple (see study note on Lev 16:2; see also [Pss 80:1](https://ref.ly/Ps80:1); [99:1](https://ref.ly/Ps99:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:16
* **Associated Articles:** Leviticus 16:2 (ID: `20161@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 37:20 (id: 16246)

you alone, O Lord, are God: Hezekiah was concerned for the Lord’s reputation, which Sennacherib had impugned. Hezekiah recognized that if the Lord defeated the Assyrians and their gods, his holy name would be glorified among all the nations of the world (cp. [Exod 9:16](https://ref.ly/Exod9:16); [14:4](https://ref.ly/Exod14:4), [17–18](https://ref.ly/Exod14:17-Exod14:18); [Josh 2:9–11](https://ref.ly/Josh2:9-Josh2:11); [1 Sam 4:7–8](https://ref.ly/1Sam4:7-1Sam4:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:20

## Isaiah 37:21 (id: 16247)

Because you prayed: Prayer is powerful; it moved God’s heart and was part of the reason why the Lord answered with this message of hope.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:21

## Isaiah 37:22 (id: 16248)

The virgin daughter of Zion: Jerusalem is personified as a young woman who mocks the Assyrian king (see also [3:26](https://ref.ly/Isa3:26)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:22

## Isaiah 37:23 (id: 16249)

Whom . . . Against whom: Sennacherib’s speech was not so much an insult against Judah as it was against the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:23

## Isaiah 37:24 (id: 16250)

Isaiah seems to be familiar with the typical royal Assyrian boasts, including that they possessed the highest mountains. These words sound very much like those found in the annals of the Assyrian kings inscribed on the walls of their temples.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:24

## Isaiah 37:25 (id: 16251)

I have dug wells . . . I stopped up all the rivers of Egypt: Sennacherib’s boasts demonstrate his attitude of independence. However, the Lord alone is sovereign over nature ([42:15](https://ref.ly/Isa42:15); [43:19](https://ref.ly/Isa43:19); [44:27](https://ref.ly/Isa44:27)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:25

## Isaiah 37:26 (id: 16252)

I planned for you to crush . . . into heaps of rubble: Assyria was God’s agent of destruction, but the Assyrians did not realize that they were only a tool in God’s hand (see ch [10](https://ref.ly/Isa10:1-Isa10:34)), fulfilling what God had planned long before ([14:24–27](https://ref.ly/Isa14:24-Isa14:27)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:26

## Isaiah 37:28 (id: 16253)

you have raged against me: Assyria was utterly hostile to the Lord, which brought them even greater condemnation.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:28

## Isaiah 37:29 (id: 16254)

my hook . . . my bit: The Assyrians would be led away like animals, just as they had led so many of their captives away.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:29

## Isaiah 37:30 (id: 16256)

This year . . . next year . . . the third year: Because of the Assyrian siege and its destruction of agriculture, the people of Judah would not be able to plant and harvest as usual. The promise that life would resume after the siege assured them that God was with them and would provide as they carried out their everyday activities. They needed to develop their trust in God over a period of three calendar years in the confident expectation that God’s word would be true (cp. [30:15](https://ref.ly/Isa30:15), [18](https://ref.ly/Isa30:18)). Perhaps the point was that after rescue from the Assyrians, it would be too late in the present year for fall planting. They would have to wait until fall in the second year to plant again, and they would reap their first crop in the spring and summer of the third year.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:30

## Isaiah 37:30–35 (id: 16255)

In this prophecy of salvation, Isaiah assured Hezekiah that Jerusalem would be spared and that the remnant was under God’s protection. The names of Isaiah and his sons anticipated God’s rescue (see [7:1–11:16](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa11:16)). The book’s record of God’s presence and rescue provided assurance that the Lord would always have a remnant that he will protect and rescue.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:30–ISA 37:35

## Isaiah 37:32 (id: 16257)

The passionate commitment of God includes his energetic zeal to keep his promise, the best guarantee his people could ever have (see also [9:7](https://ref.ly/Isa9:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:32

## Isaiah 37:33 (id: 16258)

armies . . . arrow . . . shields . . . banks of earth: Despite their advanced military technology and great power, the Assyrians would not succeed against Jerusalem.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:33

## Isaiah 37:35 (id: 16259)

for the sake of my servant David: God had promised David a perpetual dynasty (see [9:6–7](https://ref.ly/Isa9:6-Isa9:7); [2 Sam 7:8–17](https://ref.ly/2Sam7:8-2Sam7:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:35

## Isaiah 37:36 (id: 16260)

The angel of the Lord was a special heavenly agent through whom God worked on earth. Often his role was to communicate special messages (see [Gen 16:7–14](https://ref.ly/Gen16:7-Gen16:14)), but sometimes he brought judgment (see [2 Sam 24:16](https://ref.ly/2Sam24:16)). • killed 185,000 Assyrian soldiers: The Lord began to fulfill what he had repeatedly promised—rescue of Jerusalem and judgment of Assyria (see [Isa 10:16](https://ref.ly/Isa10:16), [33–34](https://ref.ly/Isa10:33-Isa10:34); [30:31](https://ref.ly/Isa30:31); [31:8](https://ref.ly/Isa31:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:36

## Isaiah 37:38 (id: 16261)

One day: Sennacherib was probably killed in 681 BC, about twenty years after his withdrawal from Jerusalem. • Esarhaddon was king of Assyria from 680–669 BC. It is ironic that Sennacherib, who mocked the Lord, was killed by his sons in the temple of his god.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 37:38

## Isaiah 38:1 (id: 16272)

About that time (literally *in those days*): This is a very general time statement. Hezekiah’s illness and subsequent healing probably preceded Jerusalem’s rescue (chs [36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa37:38)), even though Isaiah places it afterward. Merodach\-baladan’s reign in Babylon ([39:1](https://ref.ly/Isa39:1)) ended before Sennacherib’s invasion in 701 BC. The order in the text indicates a connection between these two events (see [38:6](https://ref.ly/Isa38:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:1

## Isaiah 38:1–9 (id: 16266)

The story of Hezekiah’s healing connects Hezekiah’s extension of life with Jerusalem’s relief from the Assyrians. The events of this story took place before Jerusalem’s rescue from the Assyrians but were placed in a thematic rather than chronological order. One theme is the importance of prayer (see [37:14–20](https://ref.ly/Isa37:14-Isa37:20); [38:2–3](https://ref.ly/Isa38:2-Isa38:3)). The Lord does listen! Hezekiah had learned the lesson of trust that his father, Ahaz, refused. As a result, Judah and Jerusalem were spared total destruction by the Assyrians. Yet Hezekiah was not the Messiah, the Son of David promised in [7:14](https://ref.ly/Isa7:14); [9:1–7](https://ref.ly/Isa9:1-Isa9:7); [11:1–16](https://ref.ly/Isa11:1-Isa11:16); [16:5](https://ref.ly/Isa16:5); [32:1](https://ref.ly/Isa32:1), [and 33:17–22](https://ref.ly/Isa33:17-Isa33:22). Thus, these accounts of his failure and mortality (chs [38–39](https://ref.ly/Isa38:1-Isa39:8)) prepare the reader for the revelation of the true Messiah that follows (chs [40–66](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa66:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:1–ISA 38:9

## Isaiah 38:3 (id: 16275)

I have always been faithful to you: Hezekiah’s heart was right with the Lord for most of his reign ([2 Kgs 18:1–5](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:1-2Kgs18:5)), although his dependence on Egypt was a time of weakness when he did not act faithfully ([Isa 31:1–9](https://ref.ly/Isa31:1-Isa31:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:3

## Isaiah 38:5 (id: 16276)

the God of your ancestor David: Hezekiah faithfully walked in David’s footsteps (see [37:35](https://ref.ly/Isa37:35); [38:1–22](https://ref.ly/Isa38:1-Isa38:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:5

## Isaiah 38:6 (id: 16277)

I will rescue you . . . I will defend this city: This promise refers to the rescue of Jerusalem (chs [36–37](https://ref.ly/Isa36:1-Isa37:38)); it also provides a greater context for understanding Hezekiah’s sickness and healing. Just as Hezekiah received another fifteen years, Jerusalem also received a temporary reprieve (see ch [39](https://ref.ly/Isa39:1-Isa39:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:6

## Isaiah 38:9–20 (id: 16278)

Hezekiah’s poem contains both lament ([38:9–14](https://ref.ly/Isa38:9-Isa38:14)) and praise ([38:15–20](https://ref.ly/Isa38:15-Isa38:20)), although the praise is distinctly muted until the final verse.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:9–ISA 38:20

## Isaiah 38:10 (id: 16267)

enter the place of the dead? (literally *enter the gates of Sheol?*): This image comes from an ancient idea that people enter death through gates (see [14:9](https://ref.ly/Isa14:9)). Old Testament believers did not understand the afterlife as the New Testament reveals it. It was a shadowy place where all the dead were together and where no praise of God existed (see [38:18](https://ref.ly/Isa38:18); [Pss 88:10](https://ref.ly/Ps88:10); [115:17](https://ref.ly/Ps115:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:10

## Isaiah 38:11 (id: 16268)

Hezekiah lamented that if he died, he would not enjoy fellowship with God, his family, and his friends.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:11

## Isaiah 38:12 (id: 16269)

These images depicted the brevity of life (cp. [2 Cor 5:1](https://ref.ly/2Cor5:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:12

## Isaiah 38:15 (id: 16270)

I will walk humbly: Hezekiah might have previously taken his life and good health for granted. Now he recognized that these gifts from the Lord can be withdrawn at any time.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:15

## Isaiah 38:16 (id: 16271)

God’s restoration of Hezekiah’s health also symbolized the restoration from exile that Judah would experience in the future.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:16

## Isaiah 38:21–22 (id: 16273)

Hezekiah received a sign, reminiscent of another sign the prophet had offered Ahaz ([7:11](https://ref.ly/Isa7:11)). Ahaz and Hezekiah were two bookends to a long story. Ahaz represented the ungodly king and Hezekiah the godly king. The former brought disaster on his people, while the latter rescued his people (but see ch [39](https://ref.ly/Isa39:1-Isa39:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:21–ISA 38:22

## Isaiah 38:22 (id: 16274)

Hezekiah could not go to the Temple with an infection (see [Lev 13](https://ref.ly/Lev13:1-Lev13:59)), so going to the Temple signifies restored health.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 38:22

## Isaiah 39:1 (id: 16280)

Merodach\-baladan*,* the king of Babylon (722–710 and 704–703 BC), planned to rebel against Assyria and sought help from Hezekiah. His envoys visited after Hezekiah had been very sick and had recovered but before Sennacherib’s final attack in 701 BC.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 39:1

## Isaiah 39:1–8 (id: 16279)

Hezekiah was exemplary in godliness, prayer, and care for the people of Jerusalem. But he failed the Lord by parading his financial and military strength before the envoys from Babylon.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 39:1–ISA 39:8

## Isaiah 39:2 (id: 16281)

silver . . . aromatic oils . . . his armory: Showing these treasures demonstrated to Babylon’s envoys that Hezekiah had the resources to be a worthy ally.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 39:2

## Isaiah 39:6–7 (id: 16282)

carried off to Babylon: This prophecy was given before the prophecy of the rescue of Jerusalem ([37:35](https://ref.ly/Isa37:35)). Although this pronouncement of judgment and exile comes as a surprise ending to the story of Hezekiah, the exile of both Israel and Judah had been in the background from the beginning ([10:3–4](https://ref.ly/Isa10:3-Isa10:4), [20–23](https://ref.ly/Isa10:20-Isa10:23)). The Lord had promised that he would spare Judah and Jerusalem when the Assyrians attacked in 701 BC, but he had clearly revealed to Isaiah that worse things would come if they did not change their ways. God had demonstrated the truth of his sovereignty and trustworthiness in his defeat of the Assyrians, but that did not mean the people had changed. The only way the promises of [2:1–5](https://ref.ly/Isa2:1-Isa2:5) and [4:2–6](https://ref.ly/Isa4:2-Isa4:6) were going to be realized was through the fires of judgment ([4:4](https://ref.ly/Isa4:4)). Until the burning coal of exile was applied to the nation’s unclean lips ([6:5–7](https://ref.ly/Isa6:5-Isa6:7)), their mission to the nations would fail.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 39:6–ISA 39:7

## Isaiah 40:1–31 (id: 16290)

This chapter introduces chs [40–66](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa66:24). In this section, comfort refers to the result of God’s merciful way of dealing with people in the age to come. It includes encouragement, strength, and acceptance. God wants to rescue his people ([40:1–11](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa40:11)) and is more than able to do so ([40:12–26](https://ref.ly/Isa40:12-Isa40:26)). The exiles needed to believe God’s promises and wait on him for their rescue ([40:27–31](https://ref.ly/Isa40:27-Isa40:31); see also [30:15–18](https://ref.ly/Isa30:15-Isa30:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:1–ISA 40:31

## Isaiah 40:1–55.13 (id: 16291)

This section announces the good news of God’s coming salvation. The Lord is coming to vindicate his own and to judge his enemies. Salvation would be accomplished through the arrival, suffering, and exaltation of the Lord’s servant—the Messiah, Jesus Christ.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:1–ISA 40:11

## Isaiah 40:1–66.24 (id: 16292)

The rest of Isaiah provides a message of comfort and a revelation of God’s character and his purposes for Israel. As [39:6–7](https://ref.ly/Isa39:6-Isa39:7) predicted, Judah would experience judgment and exile after the time of Isaiah. Throughout chs [40–66](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa66:24), Isaiah prophesied from the vantage point of the Exile having already become a reality. Therefore, the Babylonian exile provides the background for understanding these chapters.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:1–ISA 40:2

## Isaiah 40:2 (id: 16310)

Speak tenderly: The prophet’s message was to encourage Jerusalem with the good news of God’s forgiveness, reconciliation, and restoration. • From the prophetic perspective, Israel’s sad days are gone because the Exile is over. • punished her twice over for all her sins: Israel experienced the full brunt of God’s wrath ([51:19–23](https://ref.ly/Isa51:19-Isa51:23); cp. [61:7](https://ref.ly/Isa61:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:2

## Isaiah 40:3 (id: 16313)

the voice of someone shouting: In [Mal 3:1](https://ref.ly/Mal3:1) this person (possibly the Elijah of [Mal 4:5](https://ref.ly/Mal4:5)) was God’s messenger who prepared the way for God’s coming. In the Gospels, this announcer was identified with John the Baptist ([Matt 3:3](https://ref.ly/Matt3:3); [Mark 1:3](https://ref.ly/Mark1:3); [Luke 3:4](https://ref.ly/Luke3:4); [John 1:23](https://ref.ly/John1:23)). • Clear the way . . . Make a straight highway: The announcer’s message would expedite God’s coming (see [Isa 35:8](https://ref.ly/Isa35:8)). As God made a way for Israel through the Red Sea ([43:16](https://ref.ly/Isa43:16)), so a way would be opened up for the Lord (see [52:11–12](https://ref.ly/Isa52:11-Isa52:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:3

## Isaiah 40:3–5 (id: 16311)

There is now a shift from the general announcement of the good news to the specific expectation of God’s coming. The good news ([40:1–2](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa40:2)) is grounded in God himself. Isaiah portrayed the Lord as coming on a highway through the desert (see ch [35](https://ref.ly/Isa35:1-Isa35:10)), although the imagery is here reversed: In ch [35](https://ref.ly/Isa35:1-Isa35:10), the people were traveling to Zion, toward the Lord. Here, the Lord is coming toward his people (see also [52:8](https://ref.ly/Isa52:8), where the two images are brought together).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:3–ISA 40:5

## Isaiah 40:5 (id: 16314)

The future revelation of God’s glory would include the return of Judah from exile but would also go beyond it. The New Testament confirmed that the revelation of God’s glory came in Jesus Christ ([John 1:14](https://ref.ly/John1:14); [Heb 1:3](https://ref.ly/Heb1:3)). • The Lord has spoken: The coming salvation and judgment would certainly occur because the Lord, who keeps his word, had declared it (see [Isa 1:20](https://ref.ly/Isa1:20); [58:14](https://ref.ly/Isa58:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:5

## Isaiah 40:6 (id: 16316)

Shout: So that all can hear. • People are not worthy of the revelation of God’s glory because they are mortal.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:6

## Isaiah 40:6–8 (id: 16315)

All humanity is mortal and fleeting. By contrast, God persists in his plans and succeeds. Time does not limit him from carrying out his will (see [14:24](https://ref.ly/Isa14:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:6–ISA 40:8

## Isaiah 40:7 (id: 16317)

The breath of the Lord refers to God’s spoken words of judgment (see [4:4](https://ref.ly/Isa4:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:7

## Isaiah 40:8 (id: 16318)

the word of our God stands forever: All of God’s words (his plans as well as the written word) will succeed. God might frustrate human expectations, but everything will happen according to his wise plan (see [14:24](https://ref.ly/Isa14:24); [55:8–11](https://ref.ly/Isa55:8-Isa55:11); see also [Matt 25:34](https://ref.ly/Matt25:34)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:8

## Isaiah 40:9 (id: 16320)

God is coming to save and restore human beings (see also [35:4](https://ref.ly/Isa35:4); [Rev 22:12](https://ref.ly/Rev22:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:9

## Isaiah 40:9–11 (id: 16319)

God is the good news. Although he comes like a warrior to rescue his people with power, he also holds them tenderly as a shepherd.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:9–ISA 40:11

## Isaiah 40:10 (id: 16293)

The Lord’s rule is not like that of the unjust and powerless rulers whom he will judge. It is compassionate, just, righteous, and powerful. • with a powerful arm: God manifested his power in events such as the defeat of the Egyptians ([63:12](https://ref.ly/Isa63:12)), the judgment of his enemies ([30:30](https://ref.ly/Isa30:30); [48:14](https://ref.ly/Isa48:14); [51:9](https://ref.ly/Isa51:9)), and the rescue of his people ([59:16](https://ref.ly/Isa59:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:10

## Isaiah 40:11 (id: 16294)

Isaiah used the familiar biblical metaphor of a shepherd to speak of God’s care for his people (see also [49:10](https://ref.ly/Isa49:10); [Ps 23:1](https://ref.ly/Ps23:1); [Jer 3:15](https://ref.ly/Jer3:15); [23:4](https://ref.ly/Jer23:4); [Ezek 34:11–17](https://ref.ly/Ezek34:11-Ezek34:17); [Matt 2:6](https://ref.ly/Matt2:6); [John 10:1–18](https://ref.ly/John10:1-John10:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:11

## Isaiah 40:12–13 (id: 16295)

These rhetorical questions evoke the answer “No one!” God alone created all that exists. He will judge creation ([24:17](https://ref.ly/Isa24:17)), and he alone can and will restore creation through his salvation.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:12–ISA 40:13

## Isaiah 40:12–17 (id: 16296)

Against the backdrop of Israel’s questions (spoken by the prophet), the Lord affirmed that he is the incomparable God, a wise and sovereign creator who rules over the nations.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:12–ISA 40:17

## Isaiah 40:15 (id: 16297)

God is sovereign over all the nations of the world and over their human power structures. In comparison to God, they are almost nothing. This included the Babylonian Empire, which was merely a tool in God’s hand. • whole earth: Literally *coastlands* or *islands.*

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:15

## Isaiah 40:16 (id: 16298)

Lebanon was prized for its abundance of wood and its wildlife (see [2:13–16](https://ref.ly/Isa2:13-Isa2:16); [Ps 104:16–17](https://ref.ly/Ps104:16-Ps104:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:16

## Isaiah 40:17 (id: 16299)

The nations, including their leaders ([40:23](https://ref.ly/Isa40:23)), pagan structures ([41:24](https://ref.ly/Isa41:24),[29](https://ref.ly/Isa41:29); [44:9–11](https://ref.ly/Isa44:9-Isa44:11)), and all enemies of God’s people ([41:11–12](https://ref.ly/Isa41:11-Isa41:12)), are worth nothing apart from God.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:17

## Isaiah 40:18 (id: 16300)

To whom . . . What image: Earlier in the book, idolatry was shown to be ridiculous ([16:12](https://ref.ly/Isa16:12); [37:16–19](https://ref.ly/Isa37:16-Isa37:19)). Chapters[40–48](https://ref.ly/Isa40:1-Isa48:22) open up a much more extensive argument against idolatry. Idols are symbolic representations of gods and, at times, other religious concepts. Those who worship them don’t recognize the implication that they are man\-made trinkets. Idols are powerless ([41:7](https://ref.ly/Isa41:7), [22–24](https://ref.ly/Isa41:22-Isa41:24); [48:14](https://ref.ly/Isa48:14)), give a false sense of security ([42:17](https://ref.ly/Isa42:17)), delude people ([44:20](https://ref.ly/Isa44:20)), and lead to severe disappointment ([42:17](https://ref.ly/Isa42:17); [45:16](https://ref.ly/Isa45:16), [20](https://ref.ly/Isa45:20)). They cannot help those who care for them. In fact, they are so weak that they fall down ([40:20](https://ref.ly/Isa40:20); [41:7](https://ref.ly/Isa41:7)). In the end, idols compete for God’s glory ([42:8](https://ref.ly/Isa42:8)) and take away human dignity ([44:9](https://ref.ly/Isa44:9); see also [41:21–29](https://ref.ly/Isa41:21-Isa41:29)). • The Lord, the true God, is incomparable (see [40:25](https://ref.ly/Isa40:25); [46:5](https://ref.ly/Isa46:5); [Pss 86:8](https://ref.ly/Ps86:8); [89:6](https://ref.ly/Ps89:6)); no one could resemble him.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:18

## Isaiah 40:20 (id: 16301)

image that won’t fall down: This is a sarcastic remark; it would be embarrassing if the idol did not even have the power to remain standing (see [40:18](https://ref.ly/Isa40:18); [41:7](https://ref.ly/Isa41:7); [1 Sam 5:4](https://ref.ly/1Sam5:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:20

## Isaiah 40:21 (id: 16302)

Idolaters fail to discern who God is or to respond wisely to him. The godly in Israel would understand and see the hand of God in the unfolding story of redemption, which includes both exile and restoration ([41:20](https://ref.ly/Isa41:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:21

## Isaiah 40:22 (id: 16303)

God sits enthroned as King over all the earth (see [6:1](https://ref.ly/Isa6:1); [Pss 2:4](https://ref.ly/Ps2:4); [80:1](https://ref.ly/Ps80:1); [99:1](https://ref.ly/Ps99:1); [102:12](https://ref.ly/Ps102:12); [113:5](https://ref.ly/Ps113:5)). God is so immense and awesome that humans are like grasshoppers in comparison. • Belief in God as creator of the heavens and earth contradicts the popular Babylonian and Egyptian belief that the sun, moon, and stars represent gods. • like a curtain . . . his tent: See also [Ps 104:2](https://ref.ly/Ps104:2).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:22

## Isaiah 40:23 (id: 16304)

great people: God is unimpressed by human power and fame.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:23

## Isaiah 40:25 (id: 16305)

The Holy One is a shortened form of “the Holy One of Israel,” a common designation for God in Isaiah (see also [10:17](https://ref.ly/Isa10:17); [43:15](https://ref.ly/Isa43:15); [Ps 22:3](https://ref.ly/Ps22:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:25

## Isaiah 40:26 (id: 16306)

Look up into the heavens: Just as God directed Abraham to look into the sky ([Gen 15:5](https://ref.ly/Gen15:5)), he also directed Israel to look to the stars for encouragement (see also [Isa 51:2–3](https://ref.ly/Isa51:2-Isa51:3)). The cosmos bears witness to God’s great power and incomparable strength. The Strong One is able to strengthen the weak ([40:27–31](https://ref.ly/Isa40:27-Isa40:31); see [Ps 147:5](https://ref.ly/Ps147:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:26

## Isaiah 40:27 (id: 16307)

does not see your troubles . . . ignores your rights: The coming exile, a period when God’s powerful presence and rescue would not be seen, would prompt questions (see also [49:14](https://ref.ly/Isa49:14); [54:7](https://ref.ly/Isa54:7); [Lam 5:20–22](https://ref.ly/Lam5:20-Lam5:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:27

## Isaiah 40:28 (id: 16308)

heard . . . understood: Cp. [40:21](https://ref.ly/Isa40:21). • the everlasting God, the Creator of all the earth: Creation reveals God’s power and wisdom. He is also the Creator of the age to come, a world of righteousness, justice, and peace ([45:8](https://ref.ly/Isa45:8); [48:6–7](https://ref.ly/Isa48:6-Isa48:7); [65:17–18](https://ref.ly/Isa65:17-Isa65:18)). • He never grows weak or weary: God is not susceptible to human limitations.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:28

## Isaiah 40:29 (id: 16309)

The weak in this context are those who face persecution or oppression (such as the Babylonian exile); the Lord is their only recourse (see [49:4–5](https://ref.ly/Isa49:4-Isa49:5); [50:4](https://ref.ly/Isa50:4); [Pss 68:9](https://ref.ly/Ps68:9); [119:28](https://ref.ly/Ps119:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:29

## Isaiah 40:31 (id: 16312)

trust in the Lord: See [33:2](https://ref.ly/Isa33:2); [49:23](https://ref.ly/Isa49:23); [51:5](https://ref.ly/Isa51:5). • like eagles: This powerful image of rescue reminded readers of the Exodus event of long ago ([Exod 19:4](https://ref.ly/Exod19:4); [Deut 32:10–12](https://ref.ly/Deut32:10-Deut32:12); see also [Ps 103:5](https://ref.ly/Ps103:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 40:31

## Isaiah 41:1 (id: 16326)

The nations coming together for a trial have to listen in silence to the presentation of God’s arguments. • Bring your strongest arguments: Literally *let them find new strength,* a play on [40:31](https://ref.ly/Isa40:31). • God invited the nations to come . . . and speak*,* challenging them to refute his case.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:1

## Isaiah 41:1–7 (id: 16321)

The Lord here invited the nations to a trial where he would prove that he alone is God. They were defenseless as he declared that a king from the east ([41:2](https://ref.ly/Isa41:2)) would crush Babylon, opening the way for Israel to return.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:1–ISA 41:7

## Isaiah 41:2 (id: 16332)

This king from the east was Cyrus, the Persian king who conquered Babylon in 539 BC and permitted the Jews to return to their land and rebuild Jerusalem (538 BC; [Ezra 1:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ezra1:1-Ezra1:4)). Isaiah’s prophecies about Cyrus (see [Isa 44:28–45:13](https://ref.ly/Isa44:28-Isa45:13); [48:14–15](https://ref.ly/Isa48:14-Isa48:15)) encouraged Israel to look to the Lord as sovereign over all of human history, including the actions of great kings. • Who gives this man victory over many nations? As victorious as the conquering Cyrus was, he was still subject to the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:2

## Isaiah 41:4 (id: 16333)

each new generation from the beginning of time: God is sovereign over all things. From the beginning he has unfolded each stage of history according to his plan. • In the book of Revelation, Jesus identifies himself as the Alpha and the Omega, the First and the Last*,* the Beginning and the End ([Rev 1:8](https://ref.ly/Rev1:8), [17](https://ref.ly/Rev1:17); [2:8](https://ref.ly/Rev2:8); [21:6](https://ref.ly/Rev21:6); [22:13](https://ref.ly/Rev22:13)). • I alone am he: The Lord alone is God (see also [Isa 43:10](https://ref.ly/Isa43:10), [13](https://ref.ly/Isa43:13); [46:4](https://ref.ly/Isa46:4); [48:12](https://ref.ly/Isa48:12); [Deut 32:39](https://ref.ly/Deut32:39)). Jesus used similar expressions for himself (see [John 6:35](https://ref.ly/John6:35); [8:12](https://ref.ly/John8:12), [24](https://ref.ly/John8:24); [9:5](https://ref.ly/John9:5); [10:7](https://ref.ly/John10:7), [9](https://ref.ly/John10:9), [11](https://ref.ly/John10:11), [14](https://ref.ly/John10:14); [11:25](https://ref.ly/John11:25); [14:6](https://ref.ly/John14:6); [15:1](https://ref.ly/John15:1), [5](https://ref.ly/John15:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:4

## Isaiah 41:5–7 (id: 16334)

This taunt against idolatry was a response to God’s raising up of Cyrus; the nations hope to find protection in their idols.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:5–ISA 41:7

## Isaiah 41:6 (id: 16335)

Be strong! The nations put their hope in things that have no strength, objects made by human craftsmen.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:6

## Isaiah 41:8 (id: 16337)

The servant—here the nation of Israel—was God’s chosen one*,* whose calling was grounded in God’s purposes. • Abraham my friend: The phrase in Hebrew could mean *Abraham who loves me* or *Abraham* *whom I love.* See also [Jas 2:23](https://ref.ly/Jas2:23).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:8

## Isaiah 41:8–16 (id: 16336)

These two prophecies of salvation for God’s servant Israel ([41:8–13](https://ref.ly/Isa41:8-Isa41:13), [14–16](https://ref.ly/Isa41:14-Isa41:16)) prepare for the first of the suffering servant songs (see study note on 42:1–4).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:8–ISA 41:16
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 42:1-4 (ID: `16339@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 41:9 (id: 16338)

I have chosen you restates [41:8](https://ref.ly/Isa41:8) to emphasize God’s commitment and faithfulness to his people Israel, who had not been faithful to him. • not throw you away: Because of the Exile, the people felt that God had rejected them (see [49:21](https://ref.ly/Isa49:21); [54:6](https://ref.ly/Isa54:6)). However, God had good purposes for them.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:9

## Isaiah 41:10 (id: 16322)

I am with you: The promise of God’s presence (see [7:14](https://ref.ly/Isa7:14)) is central to the Bible. Because God is present, his people do not need to fear (see also [43:1–2](https://ref.ly/Isa43:1-Isa43:2), [5](https://ref.ly/Isa43:5)). • I am your God: The Lord used the language of the covenant to affirm that he is their God and that they are his people (see also [Jer 7:23](https://ref.ly/Jer7:23); [31:1](https://ref.ly/Jer31:1), [33](https://ref.ly/Jer31:33); [Ezek 14:11](https://ref.ly/Ezek14:11); [36:28](https://ref.ly/Ezek36:28); [37:27](https://ref.ly/Ezek37:27); [Zech 8:8](https://ref.ly/Zech8:8)). • hold you . . . my victorious right hand: The Lord used language reminiscent of the Exodus (cp. [Exod 15:6](https://ref.ly/Exod15:6)) to encourage Israel (see also [Isa 41:13](https://ref.ly/Isa41:13); [63:12](https://ref.ly/Isa63:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:10

## Isaiah 41:14 (id: 16323)

In exile, Israel was in a lowly state. • God is the Redeemer. He works mightily to rescue and restore people from sin and its consequences (for Israel, the Exile; for application to Jesus, see [Luke 2:38](https://ref.ly/Luke2:38); [21:28](https://ref.ly/Luke21:28); [Rom 3:24](https://ref.ly/Rom3:24); [1 Cor 1:30](https://ref.ly/1Cor1:30); [Gal 4:5](https://ref.ly/Gal4:5); [Eph 1:1–14](https://ref.ly/Eph1:1-Eph1:14); [Titus 2:14](https://ref.ly/Titus2:14); [Heb 9:12](https://ref.ly/Heb9:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:14

## Isaiah 41:16 (id: 16324)

When the grain is thrown into the wind, the chaff is blown away. Likewise, Israel’s enemies appeared to be strong but would easily be driven off.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:16

## Isaiah 41:18 (id: 16325)

rivers . . . fountains . . . pools of water . . . springs: These images would strike a responsive chord in an agrarian culture. They also suggest that rescue from the Exile would be a second exodus (cp. [Exod 15:27](https://ref.ly/Exod15:27); [17:6](https://ref.ly/Exod17:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:18

## Isaiah 41:21 (id: 16328)

the King of Israel: See [6:5](https://ref.ly/Isa6:5); [43:15](https://ref.ly/Isa43:15); [44:6](https://ref.ly/Isa44:6).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:21

## Isaiah 41:21–29 (id: 16327)

This trial scene develops the Lord’s case against idolatry. Idols are nothing but a human creation, whereas God is the Creator of all things. Idols cannot speak, act, accomplish anything, or save their worshipers.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:21–ISA 41:29

## Isaiah 41:25 (id: 16329)

I have stirred up a leader: The God who had planned the Exile through Babylon had already planned for Israel’s restoration from exile through Cyrus (see [41:2](https://ref.ly/Isa41:2); see also [13:17](https://ref.ly/Isa13:17)). • Although Persia is located to the east, rough terrain required those traveling to Israel and Judah to enter from the north. • I will give him victory . . . He will trample them: As Babylon was sent to trample Assyria, so Persia would trample Babylon.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:25

## Isaiah 41:26 (id: 16330)

Idols and false gods could not predict the future.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:26

## Isaiah 41:27 (id: 16331)

The messenger with good news told of God’s coming to rescue his people (see [40:9](https://ref.ly/Isa40:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 41:27

## Isaiah 42:1 (id: 16344)

who pleases me: God used similar language at Jesus’ baptism ([Matt 3:17](https://ref.ly/Matt3:17)). • I have put my Spirit upon him: Any leader might be called a servant, but the presence of the Spirit suggests a king of David’s line or a prophet like Moses (see [Num 11:17](https://ref.ly/Num11:17), [24–29](https://ref.ly/Num11:24-Num11:29)). • Establishing justice is the responsibility of a king (see [Isa 9:6–7](https://ref.ly/Isa9:6-Isa9:7); [Ps 72:1](https://ref.ly/Ps72:1)). • This king’s mission will be a greater mission to the nations than simply governing the small nation of Judah.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:1

## Isaiah 42:1–4 (id: 16339)

The servant here is not the people Israel (as in [41:8–28](https://ref.ly/Isa41:8-Isa41:28) and elsewhere in chs [41–48](https://ref.ly/Isa41:1-Isa48:22); see [49:5–6](https://ref.ly/Isa49:5-Isa49:6)) but is a royal figure who accomplishes his mission with care for people, especially for those who are hurting. The passage is the first of four songs about this servant ([42:1–4](https://ref.ly/Isa42:1-Isa42:4); [49:1–13](https://ref.ly/Isa49:1-Isa49:13); [50:4–11](https://ref.ly/Isa50:4-Isa50:11); [52:13–53:12](https://ref.ly/Isa52:13-Isa53:12)). He brings in an era of universal justice. For the connection of the servant with Jesus Christ, see [Matt 12:18–21](https://ref.ly/Matt12:18-Matt12:21).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:1–ISA 42:4

## Isaiah 42:2 (id: 16348)

shout or raise his voice: The royal servant will have calm confidence in his message and calling from God (see also [11:1–5](https://ref.ly/Isa11:1-Isa11:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:2

## Isaiah 42:3 (id: 16349)

The royal servant will be gentle with the oppressed and discouraged (see [3:15](https://ref.ly/Isa3:15); [41:17](https://ref.ly/Isa41:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:3

## Isaiah 42:4 (id: 16350)

distant lands: The nations long for justice and for instruction on bringing it about. In this regard the servant is like Moses, to whom the law was given. However, the servant is greater in that he extends justice beyond Israel to all the nations.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:4

## Isaiah 42:6 (id: 16351)

my people, Israel . . . my covenant with them (literally *a covenant for the people*): It is also possible that “the people” referred to all the peoples/nations of the earth (see [49:6](https://ref.ly/Isa49:6)). • light to guide the nations: See also [51:4](https://ref.ly/Isa51:4); [Acts 13:47](https://ref.ly/Acts13:47).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:6

## Isaiah 42:7 (id: 16352)

The servant will open the eyes of the spiritually blind (see [6:10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:10); [29:18](https://ref.ly/Isa29:18)) and free the spiritual captives from the prison of sin, in addition to those who were captive in the Babylonian exile (see [61:1](https://ref.ly/Isa61:1) with [Luke 4:18](https://ref.ly/Luke4:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:7

## Isaiah 42:8–9 (id: 16353)

I am the Lord: As Creator of the world, and as the only one who can bring about the things he predicts, the Lord alone is glorious and worthy of praise.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:8–ISA 42:9

## Isaiah 42:10–11 (id: 16340)

The various geographical regions represent the extremes of human habitation. The whole earth is called to praise God for his commitment to redeem humanity. • Kedar and Sela ([42:11](https://ref.ly/Isa42:11); see also [16:1](https://ref.ly/Isa16:1); [21:16–17](https://ref.ly/Isa21:16-Isa21:17)) were representative desert sites.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:10–ISA 42:11

## Isaiah 42:13 (id: 16341)

a mighty hero . . . a warrior: The background of this theme is God’s victory at the Red Sea during the Exodus ([Exod 15:3](https://ref.ly/Exod15:3)). • God will be victorious over all his enemies in order to rescue his people ([Isa 51:9](https://ref.ly/Isa51:9); [63:1–6](https://ref.ly/Isa63:1-Isa63:6); see also [Pss 54:7](https://ref.ly/Ps54:7); [108:9](https://ref.ly/Ps108:9); [112:8](https://ref.ly/Ps112:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:13

## Isaiah 42:14 (id: 16342)

I have long been silent . . . restrained myself: Israel had experienced God’s absence in the Exile, which came about because of their idolatrous ways ([57:11–13](https://ref.ly/Isa57:11-Isa57:13); [2 Kgs 17:6–24](https://ref.ly/2Kgs17:6-2Kgs17:24)). The godly will pray for God’s presence and renewed involvement in their situation ([Isa 64:12](https://ref.ly/Isa64:12)), and the Lord will answer their prayers because of his commitment to their redemption ([62:1](https://ref.ly/Isa62:1), [6](https://ref.ly/Isa62:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:14

## Isaiah 42:18–20 (id: 16343)

The Israelites who refused to listen to what God said and to understand what they saw God doing in history were rendered spiritually blind and deaf (see [6:9–10](https://ref.ly/Isa6:9-Isa6:10)). They had knowledge of the truth through God’s word and the prophets, but their closed minds refused to act upon it.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:18–ISA 42:20

## Isaiah 42:21 (id: 16345)

exalted his glorious law: God’s word is a witness to his righteous rule (see [1:10](https://ref.ly/Isa1:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:21

## Isaiah 42:22–25 (id: 16346)

Throughout its history, Israel’s sin ([42:24](https://ref.ly/Isa42:24)) made Israel fair game for foreign oppressors. Many nations, Assyria and Babylon in particular, became instruments God used to pour out his fury ([42:25](https://ref.ly/Isa42:25)) on his rebellious people.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:22–ISA 42:25

## Isaiah 42:25 (id: 16347)

Generation after generation of Israelites did not learn their lesson, even when God disciplined them through military defeat.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 42:25

## Isaiah 43:1 (id: 16358)

the Lord who created you: The language of creation (see study note on 40:28) was now applied to God’s formation of the nation Israel. • I have ransomed you: At the Exodus, when God brought his people out of bondage. Similarly, God planned to bring his people out of exile and back into their land. Ultimately, Jesus gave his life as a ransom for all humanity ([Matt 20:28](https://ref.ly/Matt20:28); [1 Tim 2:6](https://ref.ly/1Tim2:6); [Heb 9:15](https://ref.ly/Heb9:15)) • I have called you by name; you are mine: Despite having faced his wrath, the people of Israel are still God’s chosen people.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:1
* **Associated Articles:** Isaiah 40:28 (ID: `16308@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Isaiah 43:1–7 (id: 16354)

In this promise of salvation, the Lord addressed his plundered people in the first person.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:1–ISA 43:7

## Isaiah 43:2 (id: 16363)

Conquering forces could be compared to flooding rivers (see [8:8](https://ref.ly/Isa8:8)). • I will be with you: God is committed to being with his people to protect and care for them (see [7:14](https://ref.ly/Isa7:14); [41:10](https://ref.ly/Isa41:10); [43:5](https://ref.ly/Isa43:5); [45:14](https://ref.ly/Isa45:14)). • Israel experienced God’s judgment as the fire of oppression during the Exile.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:2

## Isaiah 43:7 (id: 16364)

One of God’s purposes in restoring his people was to display his glory to the watching world.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:7

## Isaiah 43:8–13 (id: 16365)

The Lord here called on Israel, his blind servant, to be his star witness in a mock trial against idols and false gods. Israel knew that the Lord alone is God, and the people had experienced his salvation.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:8–ISA 43:13

## Isaiah 43:10 (id: 16355)

you are my witnesses: By their very presence in exile, Israel was evidence that God is truly God. He predicted the Exile long beforehand, and now it had come to pass. God alone is the true God because he speaks and acts and controls all of history. Idols and false gods could do none of these things. Israel would later become even greater evidence because God had also predicted their redemption ([43:11–12](https://ref.ly/Isa43:11-Isa43:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:10

## Isaiah 43:14–21 (id: 16356)

The Lord assured Israel of its coming redemption from Babylon; the redemption would be modeled on Israel’s past redemption from Egypt and would be greater in some ways ([43:18](https://ref.ly/Isa43:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:14–ISA 43:21

## Isaiah 43:18 (id: 16357)

God did not want the Israelites to forget the exodus from Egypt. However, they needed to look forward in faith to the spectacular event that was about to occur rather than dwelling on the past.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:18

## Isaiah 43:21 (id: 16359)

One purpose of redemption is to honor God through the praises of the redeemed (see [1 Pet 2:9](https://ref.ly/1Pet2:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:21

## Isaiah 43:22 (id: 16360)

Israel’s history of rebellion had led them into crisis after crisis, yet their rebellion was so great that they stubbornly refused to ask God for help. When they did pray, their wickedness often caused their prayers not to be heard ([1:15](https://ref.ly/Isa1:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:22

## Isaiah 43:25 (id: 16361)

God alone can and does blot out . . . sins*,* no matter how many or how great (see also [44:22](https://ref.ly/Isa44:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:25

## Isaiah 43:27 (id: 16362)

leaders: God held Israel’s and Judah’s kings especially responsible for leading the nation into sin (see [2 Kgs 21](https://ref.ly/2Kgs21:1-2Kgs21:26)).

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 43:27

## Isaiah 44:1–5 (id: 16366)

The promise of salvation extended the usefulness of sinful Israel to another generation ([44:3](https://ref.ly/Isa44:3)). The past was bleak (see [43:22–28](https://ref.ly/Isa43:22-Isa43:28)), but the future would be a glorious new era transformed by the Spirit.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 44:1–ISA 44:5

## Isaiah 44:3 (id: 16375)

The restoration theme is connected with God’s promise to pour out the Spirit (see also [32:15–17](https://ref.ly/Isa32:15-Isa32:17); [Joel 2:28–32](https://ref.ly/Joel2:28-Joel2:32); [Acts 2:16–18](https://ref.ly/Acts2:16-Acts2:18)). • on your descendants . . . children: The next generation of Israelites offered hope.

* **Associated Passages:** ISA 44:3
